


# PREHISTORY OF INDIA BEFORE THE INDUS VALLEY CIVILIZATION
## UPSC PRELIMS GLOSSARY
## Chronological Coverage: Geological Background → Early Hominins → Palaeolithic → Mesolithic → Neolithic → Early Chalcolithic / Pre-Harappan Background

> Scope:
> This glossary covers prehistoric India before the mature Indus Valley Civilization.
> It includes the Stone Age, early humans, tools, climate, environment, rock art, hunting-gathering, domestication, agriculture, pottery, burials, early villages, and pre-Harappan background.
>
> Broad Chronology:
> 1. Geological and Quaternary Background
> 2. Lower Palaeolithic
> 3. Middle Palaeolithic
> 4. Upper Palaeolithic
> 5. Mesolithic
> 6. Neolithic
> 7. Early Chalcolithic / Pre-Harappan Background
>
> Important UPSC Focus:
> Tools, sites, subsistence, climate, rock art, domestication, agriculture, pottery, burials, regional cultures, and transition toward early village life.

---

# 1. BASIC PREHISTORY TERMS

## Prehistory
The period before written records. In India, prehistory includes the Stone Age and early farming cultures before writing became available.

## Protohistory
The period when people had writing or are mentioned by others, but their script is not fully understood. The Indus Valley Civilization is protohistoric because its script is undeciphered.

## History
The period for which written records are available and readable.

## Archaeology
The study of past human life through material remains like tools, pottery, bones, houses, burials, and art.

## Archaeological Site
A place where remains of past human activity are found.

## Excavation
Scientific digging of an archaeological site.

## Exploration
Surveying an area to find archaeological sites.

## Stratigraphy
Study of layers at a site. Lower layers are usually older than upper layers.

## Cultural Layer
A soil layer containing human-made objects or evidence of human activity.

## Lithic
Related to stone. Stone tools are called lithic tools.

## Lithic Industry
A group of stone tools made using similar techniques in a period or region.

## Tool Typology
Classification of tools based on shape, size, function, and manufacturing technique.

## Absolute Dating
Scientific dating that gives an approximate calendar age.

## Relative Dating
Dating by comparing layers, tools, or objects without giving an exact calendar age.

## Carbon Dating
A scientific method used to date organic remains like charcoal, bone, and seeds.

## Radiocarbon Dating
Another name for carbon dating.

## Thermoluminescence Dating
A dating method used for heated objects like pottery or burnt stone.

## Optically Stimulated Luminescence
A dating method used to find when sediments were last exposed to sunlight.

## OSL Dating
Short form of Optically Stimulated Luminescence dating.

## Palaeoenvironment
The ancient environment, including climate, vegetation, rivers, and animals.

## Palaeoclimate
Ancient climate.

## Geoarchaeology
Study of geological processes related to archaeological sites.

## Archaeobotany
Study of ancient plant remains.

## Archaeozoology
Study of ancient animal remains.

## Bioarchaeology
Study of human skeletal remains from archaeological contexts.

## Palaeontology
Study of ancient life forms through fossils.

## Fossil
Preserved remains or impressions of ancient plants, animals, or humans.

## Hominin
Humans and their close extinct relatives.

## Homo erectus
Early human species associated with Acheulian tools in many parts of the Old World.

## Homo sapiens
Modern human species.

## Anatomically Modern Humans
Humans with physical features similar to present-day humans.

## Hunter-Gatherer
A person or community living by hunting animals, fishing, and gathering wild plants.

## Forager
Another term for a hunter-gatherer.

## Nomadism
A mobile way of life where people move from place to place.

## Semi-Nomadic Life
A lifestyle in which people move seasonally but may return to the same camps.

## Sedentism
Settled life in one place for long periods.

## Domestication
The process by which humans control and breed plants or animals for use.

## Food Gathering
Collecting wild plants, fruits, roots, seeds, honey, and other natural food.

## Food Production
Producing food through agriculture and animal domestication.

## Neolithic Revolution
The transition from hunting-gathering to agriculture, animal domestication, pottery, and settled village life.

## Stone Age
The broad prehistoric period when stone was the main material for tools.

## Palaeolithic
Old Stone Age, associated mainly with hunting-gathering and stone tools.

## Mesolithic
Middle Stone Age, associated with microliths, hunting-gathering, fishing, and early domestication.

## Neolithic
New Stone Age, associated with agriculture, animal domestication, pottery, polished stone tools, and villages.

## Chalcolithic
Copper-Stone Age, when people used both stone and copper tools.

## Pre-Harappan
Cultures and settlements that existed before the Mature Harappan urban civilization.

## Early Farming Culture
A culture where agriculture and domestication began before cities developed.

---

# 2. BROAD PREHISTORIC CHRONOLOGY

## Lower Palaeolithic
Earliest phase of the Palaeolithic. Known for large stone tools like hand axes, cleavers, and choppers.

## Middle Palaeolithic
Middle phase of the Palaeolithic. Known for flake tools, points, scrapers, and prepared-core techniques.

## Upper Palaeolithic
Later Palaeolithic phase. Known for blades, burins, bone tools, ornaments, and early symbolic behavior.

## Mesolithic
Period after the Palaeolithic. Known for microliths, rock paintings, fishing, hunting, gathering, and early domestication.

## Neolithic
Period of early agriculture, animal domestication, pottery, ground stone tools, and village settlements.

## Early Chalcolithic
Period when copper began to be used along with stone. Some early farming cultures before Harappan urbanism were Chalcolithic.

## Pre-Harappan Farming Stage
Early village and regional cultures that later contributed to the rise of the Indus Civilization.

## Regionalization before Harappa
Development of regional cultures in north-west India, Baluchistan, Rajasthan, and Haryana before mature urban Harappan culture.

---

# 3. GEOLOGICAL AND ENVIRONMENTAL BACKGROUND

## Quaternary Period
The geological period covering the last 2.6 million years. It includes the Pleistocene and Holocene epochs.

## Pleistocene
Epoch associated with Ice Ages, early humans, large mammals, and Palaeolithic cultures.

## Holocene
Current geological epoch beginning around 11,700 years ago. Agriculture and settled life developed during the Holocene.

## Ice Age
A cold period when glaciers expanded and climate changed globally.

## Glacial Period
A colder phase of the Ice Age.

## Interglacial Period
A warmer phase between glacial periods.

## Last Glacial Maximum
The coldest phase of the last Ice Age, around 20,000 years ago.

## Holocene Climatic Optimum
A warmer and wetter phase in the early Holocene that helped vegetation, animals, and human settlement.

## Monsoon
Seasonal rainfall system crucial for Indian ecology, agriculture, and settlement.

## Palaeomonsoon
Ancient monsoon system reconstructed from geological and environmental evidence.

## Aridity
Dryness of climate.

## Semi-Arid Zone
Region with low rainfall, important for many prehistoric sites in Rajasthan and Gujarat.

## Alluvial Plain
Flat fertile land formed by river deposits.

## River Terrace
Step-like landform along a river, often preserving prehistoric tools.

## Gravel Bed
Layer of river stones and sediments where stone tools may be found.

## Laterite
Iron-rich soil or rock found in tropical regions; sometimes associated with prehistoric contexts.

## Loess
Wind-blown sediment found in some regions of the world; useful in prehistoric studies.

## Desert Margin
Edge of desert regions where prehistoric people used seasonal water and grasslands.

## Lake Basin
Depression containing a lake, often preserving prehistoric remains.

## Palaeolake
Ancient lake that may now be dry.

## Fossil Valley
Ancient river valley preserving old sediments, tools, or fossils.

## Sea-Level Change
Rise and fall of sea level affecting coastal settlements and migration routes.

## Coastline Shift
Movement of shoreline due to sea-level changes.

## Grassland
Open vegetation zone important for hunting animals.

## Woodland
Area with scattered trees and shrubs.

## Forest
Dense tree-covered area.

## Savanna
Grassland with scattered trees, supporting large herbivores.

## Faunal Change
Change in animal species over time due to climate and environment.

## Megafauna
Large animals such as elephants, rhinoceroses, wild cattle, and hippopotamus-like species.

## Extinction
Disappearance of a species.

---

# 4. HUMAN EVOLUTION AND EARLY HUMANS

## Hominin Evolution
The evolutionary development of humans and their ancestors.

## Homo habilis
Early human species known from Africa; associated with early stone tool use.

## Homo erectus
Early human species that spread beyond Africa and used Acheulian tools.

## Archaic Homo sapiens
Early forms of Homo sapiens with some modern and some archaic features.

## Homo sapiens
Modern humans.

## Out of Africa Theory
The view that modern humans evolved in Africa and spread to other parts of the world.

## Early Human Migration
Movement of early humans across landscapes in search of food, water, and shelter.

## Coastal Migration Theory
The idea that early modern humans may have moved along coastlines.

## Inland Migration
Movement through river valleys, grasslands, and inland corridors.

## Narmada Hominin
A human fossil found in the Narmada valley, important for studying early humans in India.

## Hathnora
Site in the Narmada valley where an important hominin fossil was found.

## Narmada Skull
A fossil skull fragment from Hathnora, often linked with archaic Homo sapiens or Homo heidelbergensis-like population.

## Hominin Fossil
Fossil remains of ancient humans or human ancestors.

## Human Adaptation
Ways humans adjusted to climate, food resources, landscapes, and animals.

## Cognitive Development
Development of thinking, planning, language, and symbolic behavior.

## Symbolic Behavior
Use of signs, art, ornaments, or rituals to express meaning.

---

# 5. PALAEOLITHIC AGE: GENERAL TERMS

## Palaeolithic Age
Old Stone Age. People lived mainly by hunting and gathering and used stone tools.

## Lower Palaeolithic
Earliest Palaeolithic phase, known for large core tools like hand axes and cleavers.

## Middle Palaeolithic
Phase known for flake tools and prepared-core technology.

## Upper Palaeolithic
Phase known for blade tools, bone tools, ornaments, and advanced hunting-gathering.

## Stone Tool
A tool made by shaping stone.

## Core Tool
Tool made from the main stone core after flakes are removed.

## Flake Tool
Tool made from a flake struck from a larger stone.

## Blade Tool
Long, narrow stone flake with parallel sides.

## Microlith
Very small stone tool, common in the Mesolithic but sometimes beginning earlier.

## Pebble Tool
Tool made from a rounded river pebble.

## Chopper
A simple tool with one sharp edge, made by removing flakes from one side.

## Chopping Tool
A tool with a cutting edge made by flaking both sides.

## Hand Axe
Large bifacial stone tool, usually pear-shaped or oval, used for cutting, digging, or butchering.

## Cleaver
Large tool with a broad straight cutting edge.

## Scraper
Tool used for scraping hides, wood, or other materials.

## Point
Pointed stone tool used for piercing, hunting, or cutting.

## Borer
Tool used for making holes.

## Burin
Chisel-like tool used for engraving or working bone and wood.

## Awl
Pointed tool used for piercing.

## Hammerstone
Stone used to strike another stone to make tools.

## Core
The stone piece from which flakes are removed.

## Flake
Piece removed from a core during tool-making.

## Retouch
Small finishing flakes removed from a tool edge to sharpen or shape it.

## Biface
Tool worked on both sides.

## Uniface
Tool worked on one side.

## Knapping
The process of making stone tools by striking stone.

## Flaking
Removal of flakes from a stone core.

## Percussion Technique
Tool-making by striking a core with a hammerstone.

## Direct Percussion
Hitting the core directly with a hammerstone.

## Indirect Percussion
Using an intermediate punch between hammer and core.

## Pressure Flaking
Removing small flakes by pressure rather than striking.

## Prepared Core Technique
Preparing a core in a planned way before removing flakes.

## Levallois Technique
A prepared-core technique common in Middle Palaeolithic industries.

## Acheulian Industry
Lower Palaeolithic tool tradition known for hand axes and cleavers.

## Soanian Industry
A pebble-tool tradition found mainly in the north-western subcontinent.

## Quartzite
Hard stone commonly used for Palaeolithic tools.

## Chert
Fine-grained stone used for sharp tools.

## Flint
Fine-grained stone used for tools; less common in India than quartzite and chert.

## Chalcedony
Fine stone used for blades and microliths.

## Jasper
Stone used for small tools and ornaments.

## Agate
Semi-precious stone used later for beads and small tools.

## Basalt
Volcanic rock used for some tools.

## Limestone Cave
Cave in limestone regions, sometimes preserving prehistoric remains.

---

# 6. LOWER PALAEOLITHIC AGE

## Lower Palaeolithic Age
The earliest phase of the Stone Age in India, associated mainly with Acheulian hand axes, cleavers, and large core tools.

## Acheulian Culture
A Lower Palaeolithic culture known for hand axes and cleavers.

## Early Acheulian
Early stage of Acheulian tools, usually large and roughly made.

## Late Acheulian
Later stage with more refined and symmetrical tools.

## Hand Axe Tradition
Tool-making tradition centered on bifacial hand axes.

## Cleaver Tradition
Tool tradition with broad cutting-edge tools.

## Bifacial Working
Shaping both sides of a stone tool.

## Large Cutting Tool
General term for hand axes and cleavers.

## LCT
Short form for Large Cutting Tool.

## Heavy-Duty Tool
Large tool used for cutting, chopping, digging, or butchering.

## Quartzite Tools
Tools made from quartzite, common in many Lower Palaeolithic sites.

## River Valley Sites
Many Lower Palaeolithic tools are found in river valleys and terraces.

## Open-Air Site
A prehistoric site located in open landscape rather than cave or rock shelter.

## Attirampakkam
Important Lower Palaeolithic site in Tamil Nadu, known for early Acheulian evidence.

## Isampur
Important Acheulian quarry-cum-workshop site in Karnataka.

## Hunsgi Valley
Prehistoric region in Karnataka with many Palaeolithic sites.

## Baichbal Valley
Region in Karnataka associated with Lower Palaeolithic remains.

## Pallavaram
Important early Palaeolithic site near Chennai where stone tools were discovered in the 19th century.

## Robert Bruce Foote
Geologist who discovered a hand axe at Pallavaram in 1863 and is often called the father of Indian prehistory.

## Father of Indian Prehistory
Title commonly given to Robert Bruce Foote.

## Didwana
Important prehistoric site in Rajasthan with Palaeolithic evidence.

## Singi Talav
Site near Didwana in Rajasthan with Acheulian tools.

## Belan Valley
Important prehistoric region in Uttar Pradesh with Stone Age and later cultural sequence.

## Son Valley
Important prehistoric region in central India.

## Narmada Valley
Important region for Lower Palaeolithic tools and hominin fossil evidence.

## Hathnora
Narmada valley site famous for human fossil remains.

## Bhimbetka
Rock shelter site in Madhya Pradesh with evidence from Palaeolithic to historic periods, famous for rock art.

## Adamgarh
Rock shelter site in Madhya Pradesh with prehistoric evidence.

## Nevasa
Site in Maharashtra with Palaeolithic and later cultural remains.

## Chirki-Nevasa
Palaeolithic site in Maharashtra associated with stone tool industries.

## Pravara Valley
Valley in Maharashtra associated with prehistoric cultures.

## Godavari Valley
Important region for prehistoric settlement.

## Krishna Valley
Region with prehistoric sites in peninsular India.

## Tungabhadra Valley
Important prehistoric region in Karnataka-Andhra area.

## Kortallayar Basin
River basin in Tamil Nadu associated with Attirampakkam and early Palaeolithic evidence.

## Soan Valley
Prehistoric region in present-day Pakistan, famous for pebble-tool industries.

## Soanian
Pebble-tool tradition of the north-western subcontinent.

## Potwar Plateau
Region in present-day Pakistan associated with Soanian tools.

---

# 7. LOWER PALAEOLITHIC LIFE

## Hunting
Killing wild animals for food.

## Scavenging
Using meat from animals killed by predators or natural causes.

## Gathering
Collecting wild fruits, roots, seeds, nuts, honey, and edible plants.

## Butchering
Cutting animal carcasses using stone tools.

## Large Animal Hunting
Hunting or scavenging animals like wild cattle, deer, elephants, and rhinoceroses.

## Seasonal Movement
Movement according to availability of water, plants, and animals.

## Riverine Adaptation
Using river valleys for water, stones, animals, and plant resources.

## Cave Use
Occasional use of caves or rock shelters for protection.

## Fire Use
Use of fire by early humans; direct evidence is rare in early phases but becomes clearer later.

## Raw Material Selection
Choosing stone types suitable for tools.

## Quarry Site
Place where stone was extracted for tool-making.

## Workshop Site
Place where tools were manufactured.

## Habitation Site
Place where people lived or camped.

## Kill Site
Place where animals were hunted or butchered.

## Tool Discard
Leaving used or broken tools at a site.

---

# 8. MIDDLE PALAEOLITHIC AGE

## Middle Palaeolithic Age
The phase after Lower Palaeolithic, known for flake tools, points, scrapers, and prepared-core techniques.

## Flake Industry
A tool industry dominated by flakes struck from prepared cores.

## Prepared Core
Core shaped before removing desired flakes.

## Levallois Technique
A prepared-core method used to produce flakes of planned shape.

## Scraper Dominance
Many Middle Palaeolithic tool assemblages have numerous scrapers.

## Point Tool
A pointed flake tool possibly used for hunting or piercing.

## Side Scraper
Scraper with a worked edge along one side.

## End Scraper
Scraper with a worked edge at the end.

## Notch
Tool with a concave indentation.

## Denticulate
Tool with toothed edges.

## Blade-like Flake
Long flake resembling a blade but not fully part of blade technology.

## Reduction in Tool Size
Middle Palaeolithic tools are generally smaller than Lower Palaeolithic hand axes.

## Middle Palaeolithic Adaptation
More flexible tool use, hunting, gathering, and wider ecological adaptation.

## Middle Palaeolithic in India
Found in many regions including Rajasthan, Gujarat, Maharashtra, Madhya Pradesh, Andhra Pradesh, Karnataka, and Tamil Nadu.

## Nevasa Middle Palaeolithic
Important Middle Palaeolithic evidence from Maharashtra.

## Samnapur
Middle Palaeolithic site in Narmada region.

## Patne
Site in Maharashtra with Middle and Upper Palaeolithic evidence.

## Didwana Middle Palaeolithic
Middle Palaeolithic evidence from Rajasthan.

## Belan Middle Palaeolithic
Middle Palaeolithic evidence from Belan Valley.

## Bhimbetka Middle Palaeolithic
Rock shelters with Middle Palaeolithic occupation.

## Jwalapuram
Important site in Andhra Pradesh associated with Middle Palaeolithic evidence and debates on Toba eruption impact.

---

# 9. TOBA ERUPTION AND PREHISTORIC INDIA

## Toba Eruption
A massive volcanic eruption in Sumatra around 74,000 years ago.

## Toba Ash
Volcanic ash deposits found in parts of India.

## Youngest Toba Tuff
Ash layer from the Toba eruption.

## Jwalapuram Toba Evidence
Jwalapuram in Andhra Pradesh has stone tools found above and below Toba ash, important for studying human continuity.

## Toba Catastrophe Theory
Theory that the Toba eruption caused a severe global climatic crisis and population bottleneck; its impact is debated.

## Population Bottleneck
Sharp reduction in population size.

## Continuity across Toba
Evidence from some Indian sites suggests tool-making continued before and after the eruption.

---

# 10. UPPER PALAEOLITHIC AGE

## Upper Palaeolithic Age
Later Stone Age phase associated with blades, burins, bone tools, ornaments, and greater symbolic expression.

## Blade Industry
Tool industry dominated by long narrow blades.

## Blade
Long stone flake with roughly parallel sides.

## Blade Core
Core prepared for removing blades.

## Burin
Chisel-like stone tool used for engraving or working bone, antler, or wood.

## End Scraper
Tool used for scraping hides or other materials.

## Backed Blade
Blade with one blunt backed edge and one sharp edge.

## Bone Tool
Tool made from bone.

## Antler Tool
Tool made from animal antler.

## Personal Ornament
Object worn for decoration or symbolic identity.

## Bead
Small perforated object used as ornament.

## Ostrich Eggshell Bead
Bead made from ostrich eggshell, found in some Upper Palaeolithic and later contexts.

## Ostrich Eggshell
Material used for beads and engraved objects in prehistoric India.

## Engraving
Cutting marks or designs on stone, bone, or shell.

## Symbolic Culture
Use of ornaments, art, and signs to express identity or belief.

## Upper Palaeolithic Art
Early forms of art, including engravings and possibly rock paintings.

## Patne
Important Upper Palaeolithic site in Maharashtra known for blades and ostrich eggshell beads.

## Bhimbetka Upper Palaeolithic
Rock shelter evidence for Upper Palaeolithic occupation.

## Kurnool Caves
Caves in Andhra Pradesh with Upper Palaeolithic and faunal evidence.

## Muchchatla Chintamanu Gavi
Kurnool cave site with prehistoric remains.

## Belan Valley Upper Palaeolithic
Upper Palaeolithic evidence from Uttar Pradesh.

## Baghor
Site in Madhya Pradesh with Upper Palaeolithic evidence and a stone object interpreted by some as ritual.

## Baghor Stone
Triangular stone from Baghor, sometimes interpreted as early ritual or symbolic object.

## Faunal Remains
Animal bones found at archaeological sites.

## Hunting Specialization
More planned hunting practices suggested by advanced tools.

---

# 11. MESOLITHIC AGE: GENERAL TERMS

## Mesolithic Age
The Middle Stone Age, after the Palaeolithic and before the Neolithic. It is known for microliths, rock art, fishing, hunting, gathering, and early domestication.

## Microlithic Culture
Culture characterized by very small stone tools.

## Microlith
Small stone tool, often less than 5 cm, used as part of composite tools.

## Composite Tool
A tool made by inserting several microliths into wood, bone, or antler handles.

## Backed Blade
A blade with one blunt edge and one sharp edge.

## Lunates
Crescent-shaped microliths.

## Triangle Microlith
Triangular small stone tool.

## Trapeze Microlith
Trapezium-shaped microlith.

## Point Microlith
Small pointed tool used in hunting.

## Bladelet
Very small blade.

## Geometric Microlith
Microlith with geometric shape such as triangle, trapeze, or lunate.

## Non-Geometric Microlith
Microlith without regular geometric shape.

## Hunting-Gathering Continuity
Mesolithic people continued hunting and gathering but with improved tools and broader resources.

## Broad-Spectrum Economy
Use of many food sources such as animals, fish, birds, plants, seeds, and tubers.

## Fishing
Catching fish using hooks, nets, traps, or spears.

## Fishhook
Tool used for fishing.

## Net Sinker
Stone or clay weight used with fishing nets.

## Bow and Arrow
Likely used with microlithic arrowheads.

## Trapping
Catching animals using traps.

## Small Game Hunting
Hunting smaller animals, birds, and reptiles.

## Seasonal Camps
Temporary settlements used during specific seasons.

## Semi-Permanent Settlement
Settlement occupied repeatedly or for longer periods but not fully permanent.

## Early Domestication
Beginning of animal domestication in some Mesolithic contexts.

## Dog Domestication
Dogs appear in some Mesolithic contexts, including burials.

## Ochre
Red pigment used in burials, painting, or ritual.

## Mesolithic Burial
Burial practices become more visible in Mesolithic sites.

---

# 12. MESOLITHIC SITES IN INDIA

## Bagor
Important Mesolithic site in Rajasthan, located on the Kothari River. It shows microliths, animal bones, and early evidence of domestication.

## Adamgarh
Mesolithic rock shelter site in Madhya Pradesh with rock paintings and animal remains.

## Bhimbetka
Major rock shelter site in Madhya Pradesh with Mesolithic occupation and rock paintings.

## Langhnaj
Mesolithic site in Gujarat known for microliths, burials, and animal remains.

## Sarai Nahar Rai
Mesolithic site in Uttar Pradesh known for human burials and microliths.

## Mahadaha
Mesolithic site in Uttar Pradesh known for burials and faunal remains.

## Damdama
Mesolithic site in Uttar Pradesh known for burials and settlement evidence.

## Chopani Mando
Important site in Belan Valley with Mesolithic to Neolithic transition evidence.

## Morhana Pahar
Rock shelter site in Uttar Pradesh with paintings and Mesolithic evidence.

## Lekhahia
Rock shelter site in Uttar Pradesh with Mesolithic burials.

## Birbhanpur
Mesolithic site in West Bengal.

## Teri Sites
Microlithic sites in Tamil Nadu’s red sand dune areas.

## Teri Dunes
Red sand dunes of Tamil Nadu with microlithic tools.

## Kurnool Region
Region with prehistoric caves and Mesolithic evidence.

## Pachmarhi
Rock shelter region in Madhya Pradesh with paintings.

## Raisen Region
Region in Madhya Pradesh with rock shelters and Mesolithic evidence.

## Kaimur Hills
Region with rock shelters and prehistoric paintings.

## Vindhyan Region
Important region for Mesolithic sites and rock art.

## Central Indian Mesolithic
Mesolithic culture of Madhya Pradesh and surrounding regions, rich in rock art and microliths.

## Gangetic Mesolithic
Mesolithic sites in the Ganga plains, especially burial sites in Uttar Pradesh.

## Western Indian Mesolithic
Mesolithic sites in Rajasthan and Gujarat, including Bagor and Langhnaj.

## Southern Indian Microlithic
Microlithic sites in Tamil Nadu, Karnataka, Andhra Pradesh, and Telangana.

---

# 13. MESOLITHIC LIFE AND SOCIETY

## Hunting
Important activity using microlithic tools.

## Gathering
Collection of wild plants, fruits, roots, seeds, and honey.

## Fishing Economy
Use of rivers, lakes, and wetlands for fish and aquatic resources.

## Wetland Adaptation
Use of lakes, rivers, marshes, and seasonal wetlands.

## Plant Food Use
Use of wild grains, seeds, fruits, tubers, and nuts.

## Animal Bones
Evidence of hunting, diet, and domestication.

## Wild Cattle
Hunted or managed animals in Mesolithic contexts.

## Deer
Commonly hunted animal.

## Antelope
Hunted animal in open landscapes.

## Wild Pig
Hunted animal in many Mesolithic sites.

## Tortoise
Aquatic animal used as food at some sites.

## Fish
Important food in riverine and wetland sites.

## Bird Hunting
Birds were likely hunted or trapped.

## Dog
Domesticated or semi-domesticated animal known in Mesolithic contexts.

## Circular Huts
Possible hut forms inferred from post-holes or settlement remains.

## Hearth
Fireplace used for cooking, warmth, and protection.

## Food Processing
Grinding, pounding, roasting, and cutting food.

## Grinding Stone
Stone used to grind seeds or grains.

## Quern
Large grinding stone.

## Pestle
Tool used for pounding.

## Social Group
Small band or community of hunter-gatherers.

## Band Society
Small mobile group with flexible leadership.

## Kinship Group
Group related by family ties.

## Ritual Behavior
Burials, ochre use, and symbolic objects suggest ritual practices.

## Burial Goods
Objects placed with the dead.

## Extended Burial
Body buried in stretched-out position.

## Flexed Burial
Body buried with limbs bent.

## Multiple Burial
Burial containing more than one individual.

## Ochre Burial
Burial where red ochre is used.

## Grave Orientation
Direction in which a body is placed in a grave.

## Mesolithic Health
Human bones provide evidence of diet, disease, injury, and lifestyle.

---

# 14. ROCK ART

## Rock Art
Paintings or engravings made on rock surfaces, cave walls, and shelters.

## Rock Painting
Painting made using mineral or organic pigments on rock surfaces.

## Rock Engraving
Designs cut or pecked into rock.

## Petroglyph
Rock engraving or carving.

## Pictograph
Rock painting or drawn symbol.

## Bhimbetka Rock Paintings
Famous paintings in Madhya Pradesh showing animals, hunting, dancing, rituals, and daily life.

## UNESCO World Heritage Site
Bhimbetka is a UNESCO World Heritage Site because of its long sequence of human occupation and rock art.

## Mesolithic Rock Art
Rock art often showing hunting scenes, animals, dancing, and group activities.

## Hunting Scene
Painting showing humans hunting animals.

## Dancing Scene
Painting showing group dance or ritual performance.

## Animal Figure
Representation of animals like deer, bison, elephant, tiger, boar, and antelope.

## Human Figure
Representation of people in hunting, dancing, or daily activities.

## Geometric Design
Abstract designs like circles, lines, grids, and patterns.

## Handprint
Impression or outline of a human hand on rock.

## Red Pigment
Common pigment used in prehistoric rock paintings, often from hematite or ochre.

## White Pigment
Pigment used in some paintings, often from lime or clay.

## Green Pigment
Rare pigment used in some rock paintings.

## Superimposition
One painting made over another, helping establish relative chronology.

## Style Sequence
Different artistic styles over time.

## X-Ray Style
Painting style showing internal organs or skeleton-like details, rare in Indian context but known in some rock art traditions.

## Ritual Interpretation
View that some rock art had religious or magical meaning.

## Hunting Magic
Theory that paintings were made to ensure success in hunting.

## Social Memory
Rock art may have preserved group stories, identity, or knowledge.

---

# 15. NEOLITHIC AGE: GENERAL TERMS

## Neolithic Age
New Stone Age, marked by agriculture, animal domestication, polished stone tools, pottery, and settled village life.

## Neolithic Revolution
The major change from food gathering to food production.

## Food Production
Growing crops and raising animals.

## Agriculture
Cultivation of plants for food.

## Domesticated Plant
Plant controlled and cultivated by humans.

## Domesticated Animal
Animal bred and managed by humans.

## Village Settlement
A small permanent or semi-permanent settlement based on farming and animal rearing.

## Sedentary Life
Living in one place for a long time.

## Pottery
Clay vessels made and fired for cooking, storage, and serving food.

## Handmade Pottery
Pottery shaped by hand without a wheel.

## Wheel-Made Pottery
Pottery made using a potter’s wheel, more common later.

## Ground Stone Tool
Stone tool made by grinding and polishing.

## Polished Stone Axe
A Neolithic tool used for cutting trees, woodworking, and agriculture.

## Celt
A polished stone axe or adze.

## Adze
Tool with cutting edge at right angle to handle, used for woodworking.

## Hoe
Agricultural tool used for digging or weeding.

## Sickle
Tool used for harvesting crops.

## Quern
Grinding stone used to process grain.

## Pestle
Tool for pounding grain or seeds.

## Storage Pit
Pit used to store grain or food.

## Granary
Structure used to store grain.

## Hearth
Fireplace in a house.

## Wattle and Daub
Building technique using woven branches covered with mud.

## Mud House
House made of mud walls.

## Pit Dwelling
House dug partly below ground level.

## Post-Hole
Hole in ground showing where wooden posts supported a structure.

## Neolithic Burial
Burial practices of Neolithic communities, often with pottery, tools, or animals.

## Domestication Package
Combination of farming, herding, pottery, and settled life.

## Agro-Pastoralism
Economy based on both agriculture and animal herding.

## Pastoralism
Economy based mainly on animal herding.

## Slash-and-Burn
Clearing vegetation by cutting and burning for cultivation.

## Shifting Cultivation
Cultivation in one area for some time before moving to another.

## Permanent Cultivation
Farming the same land repeatedly.

## Crop Storage
Keeping harvested crops for future use.

## Surplus
Extra food beyond immediate needs.

---

# 16. NEOLITHIC REGIONS OF INDIA

## North-Western Neolithic
Early farming cultures of Baluchistan and adjoining regions, especially Mehrgarh.

## Kashmir Neolithic
Neolithic culture of Kashmir valley, known for pit dwellings, bone tools, and animal burials.

## Ganga Valley Neolithic
Neolithic cultures of the middle Ganga and Belan valleys, associated with early rice evidence.

## Eastern Indian Neolithic
Neolithic cultures of Bihar, Bengal, Odisha, and Assam regions.

## North-Eastern Neolithic
Neolithic culture of Assam, Meghalaya, Nagaland, Manipur, and adjoining areas, often linked with ground stone tools and shifting cultivation.

## South Indian Neolithic
Neolithic culture of Karnataka, Andhra Pradesh, Telangana, and Tamil Nadu, known for ash mounds, cattle pastoralism, and polished stone axes.

## Deccan Neolithic
Neolithic cultures in the Deccan plateau, especially Karnataka and Andhra regions.

## Central Indian Neolithic
Neolithic and Mesolithic-Neolithic transition cultures of central India.

## Regional Neolithic Diversity
Different regions developed agriculture, pottery, and domestication at different times and in different ways.

---

# 17. NORTH-WESTERN NEOLITHIC AND PRE-HARAPPAN BACKGROUND

## Mehrgarh
One of the earliest farming settlements in South Asia, located in Baluchistan. It shows agriculture, animal domestication, pottery, beads, burials, and early craft specialization.

## Mehrgarh Period I
Early aceramic Neolithic phase with farming and domestication but no pottery.

## Aceramic Neolithic
Neolithic phase without pottery.

## Mehrgarh Period II
Phase with pottery, more crafts, and settled village life.

## Mehrgarh Period III
Later Neolithic-Chalcolithic phase with craft specialization and links toward early Harappan developments.

## Bolan Pass
Important route near Mehrgarh connecting the Indus plains with the Iranian plateau and Afghanistan.

## Kachi Plain
Plain in Baluchistan where Mehrgarh is located.

## Kili Ghul Muhammad
Early Neolithic site in Baluchistan with farming and pastoral evidence.

## Rana Ghundai
Pre-Harappan site in Baluchistan.

## Anjira
Pre-Harappan site in Baluchistan.

## Nal
Pre-Harappan site in Baluchistan known for painted pottery.

## Amri
Early settlement in Sindh with pre-Harappan cultural phases.

## Kot Diji
Early fortified settlement in Sindh, important for pre-Harappan developments.

## Rehman Dheri
Early planned settlement in the north-west, showing proto-urban features.

## Nausharo
Site near Mehrgarh showing transition from early farming to Harappan-related phases.

## Mundigak
Site in Afghanistan showing cultural links with early farming and pre-Harappan regions.

## Shortugai
Later Harappan trading site in Afghanistan; important for understanding north-western contacts but not pre-Harappan.

## Hakra Ware
Early pottery tradition found in the Ghaggar-Hakra region.

## Ravi Phase
Early cultural phase at Harappa before mature urbanization.

## Sothi-Siswal Culture
Early Harappan-related culture in Rajasthan, Haryana, and Punjab.

## Amri-Nal Culture
Pre-Harappan and early Harappan regional culture of Sindh and Baluchistan.

## Quetta Culture
Early farming and pottery culture of Baluchistan region.

## Togau Ware
Painted pottery tradition of Baluchistan.

## Nal Pottery
Colorful painted pottery of the Nal culture.

## Pre-Harappan Fortification
Walls and defenses at some early settlements before mature Harappan urbanism.

## Proto-Urban Settlement
Settlement showing early planning, crafts, and hierarchy before full cities.

## Early Craft Specialization
Specialized production of beads, pottery, ornaments, and tools before urban Harappa.

## Early Trade Networks
Exchange of stones, shells, metals, and agricultural goods before Harappan urbanism.

---

# 18. MEHRGARH: HIGH-YIELD TERMS

## Early Wheat Cultivation
Mehrgarh shows early cultivation of wheat.

## Early Barley Cultivation
Barley was one of the earliest crops at Mehrgarh.

## Domesticated Cattle
Cattle were domesticated and important at Mehrgarh.

## Sheep and Goat Domestication
Mehrgarh shows early rearing of sheep and goats.

## Mud-Brick Houses
Mehrgarh had houses made of mud bricks.

## Storage Compartments
Small storage units at Mehrgarh suggest surplus and planning.

## Burials at Mehrgarh
Burials often included ornaments, tools, and sometimes animal offerings.

## Grave Goods
Objects placed in graves.

## Dental Drilling
Mehrgarh has evidence of early dental drilling, showing advanced craft/medical skill.

## Bead-Making at Mehrgarh
Beads made from shell, stone, turquoise, lapis lazuli, and other materials.

## Lapis Lazuli
Blue semi-precious stone, likely obtained from Afghanistan.

## Turquoise
Semi-precious stone used for ornaments.

## Shell Ornaments
Shell objects show long-distance exchange with coastal regions.

## Female Figurines
Terracotta female figurines found in later Mehrgarh levels, often linked with fertility symbolism.

## Pottery Introduction
Pottery appeared after the earliest aceramic phase.

## Painted Pottery
Decorated pottery in later Neolithic-Chalcolithic phases.

## Copper Use at Mehrgarh
Copper objects appear in later phases, showing transition toward Chalcolithic culture.

## Mehrgarh and Harappan Origins
Mehrgarh is important for understanding the farming and village background of the Indus Civilization.

---

# 19. KASHMIR NEOLITHIC

## Kashmir Neolithic
Neolithic culture of Kashmir valley, known for pit dwellings, polished tools, bone tools, pottery, and burials.

## Burzahom
Important Neolithic site in Kashmir famous for pit dwellings, bone tools, and dog burials.

## Gufkral
Neolithic site in Kashmir with pit dwellings, agriculture, domestication, and pottery.

## Pit Dwelling
House dug into the ground, probably used for protection from cold.

## Burzahom Pit Dwellings
Underground or semi-underground houses found at Burzahom.

## Post-Holes at Burzahom
Holes indicating wooden posts used in roofing or structures.

## Bone Tools
Tools made from animal bones, common in Kashmir Neolithic.

## Bone Needle
Tool used for sewing or working hides.

## Harpoon
Barbed tool used for fishing or hunting.

## Stone Axe
Polished tool used for cutting or woodworking.

## Grey Ware
Pottery type found in Kashmir Neolithic.

## Handmade Pottery
Pottery shaped by hand.

## Dog Burial
Burzahom has evidence of dogs buried with humans or separately, suggesting special relation with dogs.

## Animal Burial
Burial of animals, possibly ritual or symbolic.

## Human Burial
Burials found in Neolithic Kashmir.

## Hunting in Kashmir Neolithic
Hunting continued along with farming and herding.

## Agriculture in Kashmir Neolithic
Cultivation of crops such as wheat, barley, and possibly lentils.

## Sheep and Goat Rearing
Important in Kashmir Neolithic economy.

## Cold Climate Adaptation
Pit dwellings and storage may reflect adaptation to cold conditions.

---

# 20. GANGA VALLEY AND BELAN VALLEY NEOLITHIC

## Belan Valley
Important prehistoric region in Uttar Pradesh with evidence from Palaeolithic to Neolithic phases.

## Koldihwa
Neolithic site in Belan Valley, often cited for early rice evidence.

## Mahagara
Neolithic site in Belan Valley with cattle pen evidence and agriculture.

## Chopani Mando
Site showing transition from Mesolithic to Neolithic in Belan Valley.

## Lahuradewa
Important site in Uttar Pradesh associated with early rice cultivation evidence.

## Jhusi
Site near Prayagraj with Neolithic-Chalcolithic evidence.

## Hetapatti
Site in the middle Ganga valley with early farming evidence.

## Chirand
Important Neolithic-Chalcolithic site in Bihar at the confluence of Ganga and Ghaghara region.

## Senuwar
Neolithic-Chalcolithic site in Bihar with rice, wheat, barley, and pulses.

## Taradih
Site in Bihar with Neolithic-Chalcolithic remains.

## Chechar-Kutubpur
Neolithic site in Bihar region.

## Rice Cultivation
A major feature of the Ganga valley Neolithic.

## Early Rice Evidence
Evidence of rice cultivation from sites like Koldihwa and Lahuradewa is important but debated in terms of exact domestication stage.

## Domesticated Rice
Rice controlled and cultivated by humans.

## Wild Rice
Naturally growing rice collected by humans.

## Cattle Pen
Enclosure for cattle, reported at Mahagara.

## Wattle-and-Daub Houses
Houses made of wooden framework plastered with mud.

## Cord-Impressed Pottery
Pottery decorated with impressions of cords.

## Burnished Pottery
Pottery polished to create a smooth surface.

## Neolithic-Chalcolithic Transition
Shift from stone-based Neolithic to copper-using farming cultures.

---

# 21. EASTERN INDIAN NEOLITHIC

## Eastern Neolithic
Neolithic cultures of Bihar, Bengal, Odisha, and Assam-related zones.

## Chirand
Important site in Bihar with bone tools, pottery, agriculture, and animal remains.

## Bone Tools at Chirand
Chirand is known for a rich bone tool industry.

## Antler Tools
Tools made from deer antlers, found at some eastern sites.

## Rice-Based Economy
Rice was important in eastern Neolithic and later cultures.

## Fishing and River Resources
Eastern sites often used riverine resources like fish, turtles, and aquatic animals.

## Black-and-Red Ware
Pottery type found in some later Neolithic-Chalcolithic contexts.

## Cord-Impressed Ware
Pottery decorated by pressing cord onto wet clay.

## Ground Stone Axe
Common Neolithic tool in eastern India.

## Shouldered Axe
A type of ground stone axe common in north-eastern and eastern Neolithic contexts.

## Eastern India and Southeast Asian Links
Some tool types, especially shouldered celts, are discussed in relation to links with Southeast Asia.

---

# 22. NORTH-EASTERN NEOLITHIC

## North-Eastern Neolithic
Neolithic culture of north-east India, known for polished stone tools, shouldered axes, cord-impressed pottery, and shifting cultivation.

## Daojali Hading
Important Neolithic site in Assam with polished stone tools and cord-impressed pottery.

## Sarutaru
Neolithic site in Assam region.

## Marakdola
Neolithic site in Assam.

## Meghalaya Neolithic
Neolithic remains in Meghalaya, often associated with polished stone tools.

## Nagaland Neolithic
Neolithic evidence from Nagaland region.

## Manipur Neolithic
Neolithic evidence in Manipur region.

## Shouldered Celt
Polished stone axe with a shoulder-like projection, common in north-east India.

## Ground Stone Tool
Polished stone tool used for cutting, farming, or woodworking.

## Cord-Impressed Pottery
Pottery with cord marks, common in north-eastern Neolithic.

## Shifting Cultivation
Cultivation by clearing and burning vegetation, then moving after soil fertility declines.

## Jhum Cultivation
North-eastern form of shifting cultivation.

## Austroasiatic Links
Some scholars discuss linguistic-cultural links between eastern India, north-east India, and Southeast Asia.

## Southeast Asian Affinities
Similarities in tools and pottery between north-east India and Southeast Asia.

---

# 23. SOUTH INDIAN NEOLITHIC

## South Indian Neolithic
Neolithic culture of Karnataka, Andhra Pradesh, Telangana, and Tamil Nadu, known for cattle pastoralism, ash mounds, polished stone axes, and millet cultivation.

## Ash Mound Culture
South Indian Neolithic culture known for large mounds of burnt cattle dung and ash.

## Ash Mound
Large mound formed by repeated burning of cattle dung, possibly linked with cattle rituals or seasonal gatherings.

## Cattle Pastoralism
Economy centered on cattle herding.

## Neolithic Cattle Pens
Enclosures used for keeping cattle.

## Millets
Small grains important in South Indian Neolithic agriculture.

## Horsegram
Pulse crop associated with South Indian Neolithic.

## Green Gram
Pulse crop known from some Neolithic contexts.

## Brown Top Millet
Millet crop associated with South Indian Neolithic agriculture.

## Polished Stone Axe
Common tool in South Indian Neolithic.

## Dolerite
Hard stone used for making polished axes.

## Grinding Stone
Used for processing grains.

## Handmade Pottery
Common pottery in South Indian Neolithic.

## Grey Ware
Pottery type found in South Indian Neolithic.

## Burnished Grey Ware
Polished grey pottery.

## Tekkalakota
Important Neolithic site in Karnataka.

## Brahmagiri
Site in Karnataka with Neolithic, Megalithic, and later remains.

## Maski
Site in Karnataka with Neolithic and later historical importance.

## Hallur
Important Neolithic site in Karnataka with evidence of crops and animal domestication.

## Piklihal
Neolithic site in Karnataka/Andhra region associated with cattle pastoralism.

## Utnur
South Indian Neolithic ash mound site.

## Kupgal
Important site in Karnataka with Neolithic occupation and rock art/rock bruising.

## Budihal
Important Neolithic site in Karnataka with evidence of cattle penning and settlement organization.

## Sanganakallu
Major Neolithic site complex in Karnataka.

## Watgal
Neolithic site in Karnataka.

## Tekkalkota
Alternative spelling of Tekkalakota.

## Paiyampalli
Neolithic site in Tamil Nadu with evidence of agriculture and domestication.

## Nagarjunakonda Neolithic
Prehistoric evidence in Andhra region before later Buddhist phases.

## South Indian Rock Bruising
Pecked or bruised rock surfaces, sometimes associated with Neolithic ritual or communication.

## Cattle Ritual
Possible ritual activity connected with ash mounds and cattle herding.

## Seasonal Aggregation
Periodic gathering of groups at ash mound sites.

---

# 24. NEOLITHIC CROPS

## Wheat
Early cultivated crop, especially in north-western Neolithic.

## Barley
Major early crop in Mehrgarh and north-western farming cultures.

## Rice
Important crop in the Ganga valley, eastern India, and later other regions.

## Millet
Small-grained crop important in semi-arid regions and South India.

## Browntop Millet
Important South Indian millet.

## Kodo Millet
Millet crop known in later prehistoric contexts.

## Little Millet
Small millet cultivated in peninsular India.

## Foxtail Millet
Millet crop known from later prehistoric contexts.

## Horsegram
Pulse crop important in South Indian Neolithic.

## Green Gram
Pulse crop.

## Black Gram
Pulse crop.

## Lentil
Pulse cultivated in north-western and Ganga valley contexts.

## Pea
Pulse crop.

## Chickpea
Pulse crop.

## Sesame
Oilseed crop.

## Flax
Fiber and oilseed crop known in some early farming contexts.

## Cotton
Important later in the north-west and Harappan context; pre-Harappan background includes early plant-use developments.

## Date
Fruit known in north-western regions.

## Jujube
Wild or cultivated fruit used in prehistoric contexts.

## Tuber
Underground plant food gathered or cultivated.

## Wild Grain
Naturally growing grain collected before domestication.

## Domesticated Grain
Grain produced through controlled cultivation.

## Harvesting
Cutting crops.

## Storage
Keeping crops for future use.

## Sickle Gloss
Shiny polish on tools caused by cutting grasses or cereals.

---

# 25. NEOLITHIC ANIMALS

## Cattle
Important domesticated animal in many Neolithic cultures.

## Zebu
Humped cattle common in South Asia.

## Sheep
Domesticated animal used for meat, wool, and milk.

## Goat
Domesticated animal used for milk, meat, and hide.

## Pig
Domesticated or hunted animal.

## Dog
Domesticated animal used for hunting, guarding, and companionship.

## Buffalo
Domesticated or wild animal in some regions.

## Fowl
Domestic chicken appears in later prehistoric contexts.

## Wild Cattle
Hunted and possibly managed in early contexts.

## Deer
Common hunted animal.

## Antelope
Hunted animal in dry grasslands.

## Wild Boar
Hunted animal.

## Elephant
Known wild animal, sometimes hunted or symbolically important.

## Rhinoceros
Wild animal in some prehistoric environments.

## Fish
Important riverine and lake resource.

## Turtle
Aquatic animal consumed at some prehistoric sites.

## Mollusc
Shellfish used as food or ornaments.

## Animal Domestication
Control and breeding of animals.

## Herding
Managing groups of animals.

## Pastoral Camp
Camp used by herders.

## Cattle Pen
Enclosure for cattle.

## Dung Burning
Burning animal dung, important in South Indian ash mounds.

## Milk Use
Use of milk from domesticated animals.

## Meat Consumption
Eating meat from hunted or domesticated animals.

---

# 26. NEOLITHIC POTTERY

## Pottery
Clay vessels made and fired for cooking, storage, and serving.

## Handmade Pottery
Pottery shaped by hand.

## Wheel-Made Pottery
Pottery made on a wheel, more common in later periods.

## Coiled Pottery
Pottery made by stacking clay coils.

## Burnished Pottery
Pottery polished before firing.

## Painted Pottery
Pottery decorated with painted designs.

## Cord-Impressed Pottery
Pottery decorated with cord marks.

## Incised Pottery
Pottery decorated by cutting designs.

## Black-and-Red Ware
Pottery fired to produce black interior/top and red exterior/body.

## Grey Ware
Grey-colored pottery.

## Red Ware
Red-colored pottery.

## Ochre-Coloured Pottery
Later pottery tradition of north India, not strictly Neolithic but important in prehistoric-early historic transition.

## Storage Jar
Large vessel used to store grain or water.

## Cooking Pot
Pot used for cooking.

## Bowl
Open vessel used for food or serving.

## Dish
Flat or shallow vessel.

## Spouted Vessel
Pottery with a spout for pouring.

## Pottery Kiln
Structure used for firing pots.

## Open Firing
Firing pottery in open fire rather than kiln.

## Ceramic Technology
Knowledge of clay selection, shaping, drying, and firing.

---

# 27. NEOLITHIC TOOLS AND TECHNOLOGY

## Polished Stone Axe
Important Neolithic tool used for clearing forests and woodworking.

## Celt
Polished axe or adze.

## Adze
Woodworking tool with transverse cutting edge.

## Hoe
Tool used for digging soil.

## Sickle
Harvesting tool.

## Blade
Stone tool used for cutting.

## Microlith
Small stone tool that continued into Neolithic contexts.

## Quern
Grinding stone.

## Muller
Hand-held stone used with quern for grinding.

## Pestle
Tool used for pounding.

## Mortar
Stone or wooden container used for pounding.

## Bone Point
Pointed bone tool.

## Bone Needle
Needle made from bone.

## Harpoon
Barbed tool for fishing or hunting.

## Awl
Tool for piercing leather or cloth.

## Drill
Tool used to make holes in beads or other objects.

## Bead
Small ornament with a hole.

## Spindle Whorl
Small object used for spinning thread.

## Weaving
Making cloth from thread.

## Basketry
Making baskets from reeds, grass, or plant fiber.

## Matting
Making mats from plant material.

## Woodworking
Craft of shaping wood for houses, tools, and handles.

## Ground Stone Technology
Making tools by grinding and polishing stone.

## Hafting
Attaching a stone tool to a wooden or bone handle.

## Composite Tool
Tool made of multiple parts, such as microliths inserted into wood.

---

# 28. NEOLITHIC SETTLEMENT AND ARCHITECTURE

## Village
Small settled community.

## Hamlet
Very small settlement.

## Seasonal Settlement
Settlement used during particular seasons.

## Permanent Settlement
Settlement occupied throughout the year.

## Pit Dwelling
House partly below ground level, common in Kashmir Neolithic.

## Hut
Small simple house made of wood, grass, mud, or reeds.

## Wattle and Daub House
House made with wooden framework covered with mud.

## Mud-Brick House
House made with sun-dried mud bricks.

## Stone Foundation
Base of a house made from stone.

## Post-Hole
Hole indicating wooden post used in house construction.

## Hearth
Fireplace.

## Storage Pit
Pit for storing grain or food.

## Granary
Storage structure for grain.

## Courtyard
Open space near or inside a house.

## Cattle Pen
Enclosure for livestock.

## Fence
Boundary made of wood, reeds, or branches.

## Settlement Planning
Arrangement of houses, storage spaces, work areas, and animal enclosures.

## Domestic Space
Area used for household activities.

## Work Area
Place used for tool-making, pottery, food processing, or craft.

## Refuse Pit
Pit used for waste disposal.

## Ash Deposit
Layer of ash from fires, cooking, or dung burning.

---

# 29. NEOLITHIC BURIAL AND RITUAL

## Burial
Placing the dead in the ground.

## Primary Burial
Burial of the body soon after death.

## Secondary Burial
Burial of bones after earlier exposure or temporary burial.

## Extended Burial
Body laid straight.

## Flexed Burial
Body placed with bent limbs.

## Pit Burial
Burial in a pit.

## Pot Burial
Burial in a pot or urn.

## Animal Burial
Burial of animals, known at sites like Burzahom.

## Dog Burial
Special burial of dogs, seen at Burzahom.

## Grave Goods
Objects placed with the dead.

## Beads in Burial
Ornaments placed with the dead.

## Pottery in Burial
Pots placed with the dead.

## Tool in Burial
Stone or bone tools placed in graves.

## Ochre Use
Use of red pigment in burial or ritual.

## Ancestor Ritual
Possible rituals connected with the dead.

## Fertility Figurines
Female figurines often interpreted as fertility symbols, though interpretation is debated.

## Terracotta Figurine
Small baked clay figure.

## Ritual Object
Object used for symbolic or religious purpose.

## Totemism
Belief linking a group with an animal, plant, or natural object; sometimes suggested but hard to prove.

## Shamanism
Religious practice involving ritual specialists and spirit communication; sometimes used cautiously in prehistoric interpretation.

---

# 30. MESOLITHIC-NEOLITHIC TRANSITION

## Transition to Food Production
Gradual shift from hunting-gathering to agriculture and domestication.

## Continuity of Microliths
Microliths continued to be used even after farming began.

## Mixed Economy
Economy combining hunting, gathering, fishing, farming, and herding.

## Sedentism before Agriculture
Some communities may have become semi-settled before full agriculture.

## Experimentation with Plants
Early use, management, or cultivation of wild plants.

## Animal Management
Control of animals before full domestication.

## Seasonal Herding
Movement with animals according to season.

## Early Village Life
Beginning of permanent settlements.

## Storage and Sedentism
Food storage encouraged settled life.

## Population Growth
Food production supported larger populations.

## Social Differentiation
Small differences in wealth or status began to appear in some communities.

## Craft Specialization
Some people began to specialize in pottery, beads, tools, or other crafts.

## Exchange Networks
Movement of raw materials and goods between regions.

---

# 31. EARLY CHALCOLITHIC AND PRE-HARAPPAN CULTURES

## Chalcolithic
Copper-Stone Age, when copper and stone tools were both used.

## Early Chalcolithic
Early phase of copper use before large urban civilizations.

## Copper
Metal used for tools, ornaments, and small objects.

## Native Copper
Naturally occurring copper that can be hammered into shape.

## Copper Smelting
Extracting copper from ore using heat.

## Metallurgy
Technology of working metals.

## Stone-Copper Economy
Economy using both stone and copper tools.

## Pre-Harappan Chalcolithic
Early copper-using cultures that preceded mature Harappan urbanism.

## Regional Culture
A culture limited to a particular region with its own pottery, tools, and settlement pattern.

## Proto-Harappan
Cultures showing features that later developed into Harappan civilization.

## Early Harappan
The phase before Mature Harappan urbanism. It is often protohistoric rather than purely prehistoric but belongs to the background of the Indus Civilization.

## Amri Culture
Early culture in Sindh preceding Mature Harappan development.

## Kot Diji Culture
Early fortified culture in Sindh and surrounding areas.

## Sothi-Siswal Culture
Early culture in Rajasthan, Haryana, and Punjab linked with pre-Harappan development.

## Hakra Culture
Early culture of the Ghaggar-Hakra region.

## Ravi Culture
Early phase at Harappa before Mature Harappan civilization.

## Nal Culture
Pre-Harappan culture in Baluchistan known for painted pottery.

## Togau Culture
Early pottery culture of Baluchistan.

## Quetta Culture
Pre-Harappan culture of Baluchistan.

## Early Fortified Settlement
Settlement with defensive or boundary walls before urban Harappan cities.

## Early Urban Traits
Planning, craft specialization, trade, storage, and social hierarchy before mature cities.

## Surplus Storage
Storage of extra grain or goods.

## Long-Distance Exchange
Movement of stones, metals, shells, and ornaments across regions.

## Social Complexity
Increasing differences in occupation, wealth, status, and settlement size.

---

# 32. PREHISTORIC REGIONAL SEQUENCES

## North-West Sequence
Mehrgarh → Early farming villages → Regional Chalcolithic cultures → Early Harappan → Mature Harappan.

## Kashmir Sequence
Neolithic pit dwellings and bone tools → Later Neolithic-Chalcolithic developments.

## Ganga Valley Sequence
Mesolithic hunter-gatherers → Neolithic rice-using villages → Chalcolithic farming cultures.

## Central India Sequence
Palaeolithic tools → Mesolithic rock shelters and microliths → Neolithic-Chalcolithic transition.

## Western India Sequence
Palaeolithic and Mesolithic sites → Early pastoralism and microliths → Chalcolithic cultures.

## South India Sequence
Palaeolithic → Mesolithic microliths → Neolithic ash mound cattle pastoralism → Iron Age megalithic cultures later.

## North-East Sequence
Neolithic ground stone tools and cord-impressed pottery → Later farming and shifting cultivation cultures.

## Peninsular Sequence
Long use of stone tools followed by Neolithic pastoral-farming communities.

---

# 33. IMPORTANT PREHISTORIC SITES: QUICK GLOSSARY

## Pallavaram
Early Palaeolithic site near Chennai where Robert Bruce Foote found a hand axe.

## Attirampakkam
Major Lower Palaeolithic site in Tamil Nadu with early Acheulian evidence.

## Hunsgi
Important Palaeolithic region in Karnataka.

## Isampur
Acheulian quarry-workshop site in Karnataka.

## Bhimbetka
Rock shelter site in Madhya Pradesh with long prehistoric occupation and rock paintings.

## Adamgarh
Rock shelter site in Madhya Pradesh with Mesolithic paintings and animal remains.

## Hathnora
Narmada valley site where important hominin fossil was found.

## Didwana
Prehistoric site in Rajasthan with Palaeolithic evidence.

## Singi Talav
Acheulian site near Didwana.

## Nevasa
Prehistoric site in Maharashtra.

## Patne
Upper Palaeolithic site in Maharashtra with blades and ostrich eggshell beads.

## Baghor
Upper Palaeolithic site in Madhya Pradesh with symbolic stone object.

## Jwalapuram
Site in Andhra Pradesh important for Middle Palaeolithic and Toba ash evidence.

## Kurnool Caves
Cave sites in Andhra Pradesh with prehistoric tools and faunal remains.

## Bagor
Mesolithic site in Rajasthan with microliths and early domestication evidence.

## Langhnaj
Mesolithic site in Gujarat with microliths and burials.

## Sarai Nahar Rai
Mesolithic burial site in Uttar Pradesh.

## Mahadaha
Mesolithic site in Uttar Pradesh with burials.

## Damdama
Mesolithic site in Uttar Pradesh with burials and settlement remains.

## Chopani Mando
Belan Valley site showing Mesolithic-Neolithic transition.

## Koldihwa
Neolithic site in Uttar Pradesh associated with early rice evidence.

## Mahagara
Neolithic site in Uttar Pradesh with cattle pen evidence.

## Lahuradewa
Important early rice site in Uttar Pradesh.

## Mehrgarh
Early farming site in Baluchistan, crucial for pre-Harappan background.

## Kili Ghul Muhammad
Early Neolithic site in Baluchistan.

## Nausharo
Site showing transition from Mehrgarh-like farming to Harappan-related phases.

## Burzahom
Neolithic site in Kashmir with pit dwellings and dog burials.

## Gufkral
Neolithic site in Kashmir.

## Chirand
Neolithic-Chalcolithic site in Bihar known for bone tools and riverine adaptation.

## Senuwar
Neolithic-Chalcolithic site in Bihar.

## Daojali Hading
Neolithic site in Assam with polished tools and cord-impressed pottery.

## Tekkalakota
South Indian Neolithic site in Karnataka.

## Brahmagiri
Karnataka site with Neolithic and later cultural sequence.

## Hallur
South Indian Neolithic site with crop and animal evidence.

## Piklihal
South Indian Neolithic cattle pastoral site.

## Utnur
Ash mound site in South India.

## Budihal
South Indian Neolithic site with cattle penning evidence.

## Sanganakallu
Major Neolithic complex in Karnataka.

## Paiyampalli
Neolithic site in Tamil Nadu.

---

# 34. IMPORTANT PREHISTORIC TOOL INDUSTRIES

## Acheulian Industry
Lower Palaeolithic industry known for hand axes and cleavers.

## Soanian Industry
Pebble-tool industry of the north-western subcontinent.

## Flake Industry
Middle Palaeolithic industry dominated by flakes.

## Blade Industry
Upper Palaeolithic industry dominated by blades.

## Microlithic Industry
Mesolithic industry dominated by small tools.

## Ground Stone Industry
Neolithic industry of polished axes, adzes, and grinding stones.

## Bone Tool Industry
Industry using bone tools, important in Kashmir and Chirand.

## Copper-Stone Industry
Chalcolithic industry using copper and stone tools.

## Quarry-Workshop Industry
Tool-making tradition near raw material sources.

## Composite Tool Industry
Use of small stone pieces inserted into handles.

---

# 35. SUBSISTENCE EVOLUTION

## Hunting-Gathering
Earliest subsistence pattern based on wild animals and plants.

## Scavenging
Use of carcasses from animals killed by others or natural causes.

## Fishing
Important in Mesolithic and riverine Neolithic contexts.

## Broad-Spectrum Foraging
Use of diverse plants and animals.

## Plant Management
Human protection or encouragement of useful wild plants before domestication.

## Animal Management
Control of animals before full domestication.

## Domestication
Human control over reproduction and use of plants or animals.

## Farming
Cultivation of crops.

## Herding
Management of domesticated animals.

## Agro-Pastoral Economy
Combination of farming and herding.

## Surplus Production
Production of extra food, allowing storage, population growth, and craft specialization.

## Exchange Economy
Movement of goods between groups.

## Craft Economy
Production of specialized objects.

## Village Economy
Economy based on farming, herding, crafts, and exchange.

---

# 36. PREHISTORIC ENVIRONMENT AND ADAPTATION

## River Valley Adaptation
Settlement near rivers for water, food, stone, and transport.

## Rock Shelter Adaptation
Use of natural shelters for habitation and painting.

## Desert Adaptation
Use of seasonal water sources and mobile life in arid zones.

## Forest Adaptation
Use of forest foods, animals, timber, and shelter.

## Grassland Adaptation
Hunting of grazing animals.

## Mountain Adaptation
Use of valleys, caves, and seasonal resources in hill regions.

## Coastal Adaptation
Use of fish, shellfish, and marine resources.

## Wetland Adaptation
Use of marshes, lakes, fish, birds, and aquatic plants.

## Seasonal Mobility
Moving according to seasonal availability of food and water.

## Resource Scheduling
Planning when and where to collect different resources.

## Risk Reduction
Using varied food sources to survive climate uncertainty.

## Storage Strategy
Storing food to survive lean seasons.

---

# 37. PREHISTORIC ART AND SYMBOLISM

## Symbolism
Use of objects, colors, art, or rituals to represent ideas.

## Body Decoration
Use of beads, pigments, ornaments, or tattoos.

## Beads
Personal ornaments showing identity or status.

## Ostrich Eggshell Beads
Upper Palaeolithic and later ornaments.

## Shell Beads
Ornaments made from shell.

## Stone Beads
Ornaments made from stone.

## Bone Ornaments
Decorative objects made from bone.

## Ochre
Red pigment used in art, burial, or body decoration.

## Rock Painting
Major form of prehistoric expression.

## Animal Symbolism
Use of animals in art or ritual.

## Fertility Symbolism
Possible symbolic importance of female figurines or agricultural themes.

## Ancestor Symbolism
Possible ritual connection with the dead.

## Ritual Space
Place used for symbolic or ceremonial activities.

## Sacred Landscape
Landscape with repeated ritual or symbolic use, such as painted rock shelters.

---

# 38. PREHISTORIC BURIALS AND BELIEFS

## Burial Practice
Treatment of the dead.

## Grave
Place where a dead body is buried.

## Cemetery
Group of burials.

## Grave Goods
Objects placed with the dead.

## Human Skeleton
Source for studying health, diet, age, and burial practice.

## Burial Orientation
Direction in which the body is placed.

## Flexed Burial
Body placed in bent posture.

## Extended Burial
Body placed straight.

## Multiple Burial
Burial of more than one person.

## Secondary Burial
Reburial of selected bones.

## Pot Burial
Burial in a pot.

## Animal Burial
Burial of animals with or near humans.

## Dog Burial
Special burial known from Burzahom.

## Red Ochre Burial
Burial using red pigment.

## Mortuary Ritual
Rituals connected with death.

## Ancestor Veneration
Respect or worship of ancestors, inferred cautiously from burials.

---

# 39. PREHISTORIC SOCIETY

## Band
Small mobile hunter-gatherer group.

## Tribe
Larger social group with shared identity, more visible in later settled contexts.

## Kinship
Family and clan relations.

## Egalitarian Society
Society with limited social inequality, common in many hunter-gatherer groups.

## Social Differentiation
Emergence of differences in wealth, occupation, status, or ritual role.

## Gender Division of Labour
Different work roles for men and women; exact prehistoric details are inferred cautiously.

## Age-Based Roles
Different duties based on age.

## Group Cooperation
Cooperation in hunting, gathering, child-rearing, and defense.

## Sharing Economy
Food and resources shared within small communities.

## Ritual Specialist
Person possibly responsible for healing, ritual, or symbolic practices.

## Craft Specialist
Person skilled in making tools, pottery, beads, or ornaments.

## Exchange Partner
Group or person involved in trade or exchange.

## Marriage Network
Social links between groups through marriage.

## Conflict
Violence or competition over resources, visible rarely through skeletal injuries or site evidence.

---

# 40. PREHISTORIC ECONOMY AND EXCHANGE

## Raw Material Procurement
Obtaining stone, clay, wood, bone, shell, or metal for tools and objects.

## Local Raw Material
Material found near a settlement.

## Non-Local Raw Material
Material brought from distant areas.

## Exchange Network
System through which goods moved between groups.

## Gift Exchange
Exchange to build social relations.

## Barter
Exchange of one good for another.

## Long-Distance Exchange
Movement of materials such as shell, lapis lazuli, turquoise, or special stone over long distances.

## Shell Exchange
Movement of marine shells inland for ornaments.

## Stone Exchange
Movement of good-quality tool stone.

## Bead Exchange
Movement of ornaments between communities.

## Craft Production
Making tools, pottery, ornaments, or other goods.

## Specialized Production
Production by skilled individuals or groups.

## Household Production
Production done within families.

## Workshop
Place where tools or crafts are made.

---

# 41. PREHISTORIC TECHNOLOGICAL CHANGE

## Stone Tool Technology
Techniques for making stone tools.

## Fire Technology
Use of fire for cooking, warmth, protection, and later pottery and metallurgy.

## Pottery Technology
Making clay vessels by shaping and firing.

## Grinding Technology
Using grinding stones to process seeds and grains.

## Polishing Technology
Grinding and smoothing stone tools.

## Drilling Technology
Making holes in beads, teeth, or ornaments.

## Weaving Technology
Making cloth or mats from fibers.

## Basketry Technology
Making baskets from plant materials.

## Metallurgical Beginnings
Early use of copper before large-scale metallurgy.

## Heat Treatment
Heating stone to improve flaking quality, known in some prehistoric contexts.

## Hafting Technology
Attaching stone tools to handles.

## Composite Technology
Combining several materials in one tool.

---

# 42. PREHISTORIC CLIMATE AND CULTURAL CHANGE

## Climate Change
Long-term change in rainfall, temperature, and environment.

## Monsoon Strengthening
Increase in rainfall during certain Holocene phases, supporting vegetation and settlement.

## Monsoon Weakening
Decrease in rainfall causing aridity and settlement changes.

## Dry Phase
Period of reduced rainfall.

## Wet Phase
Period of increased rainfall.

## Environmental Stress
Pressure caused by climate, resource shortage, or population growth.

## Adaptation Strategy
Human response to changing environment.

## Mobility Strategy
Movement to use different resources.

## Intensification
Increased use of certain resources, such as grains or fish.

## Domestication as Adaptation
Farming and herding as responses to changing food needs and environment.

## Regional Diversity
Different areas changed at different speeds and in different ways.

---

# 43. IMPORTANT PREHISTORIC LIMITS AND TRANSITIONS

## Palaeolithic to Mesolithic Transition
Shift from large tools to microliths and broader resource use.

## Mesolithic to Neolithic Transition
Shift from hunting-gathering to farming and herding.

## Neolithic to Chalcolithic Transition
Introduction of copper along with stone tools.

## Pre-Harappan to Harappan Transition
Growth of early farming villages and regional cultures into urban Harappan civilization.

## Food Gathering to Food Production
One of the most important transitions in human history.

## Mobile Camps to Villages
Shift from temporary camps to settled communities.

## Simple Tools to Specialized Tools
Increasing diversity and specialization of tools.

## Local Exchange to Long-Distance Trade
Expansion of exchange networks over time.

## Kin Groups to Complex Communities
Growth of larger and more organized settlements.

---

# 44. CULTURAL PERIODS: QUICK COMPARISON

## Lower Palaeolithic
Large stone tools; hand axes and cleavers; hunting-gathering; river valleys.

## Middle Palaeolithic
Flake tools; scrapers and points; prepared-core technology; wider adaptation.

## Upper Palaeolithic
Blade tools; bone tools; ornaments; symbolic behavior; modern humans.

## Mesolithic
Microliths; hunting, gathering, fishing; rock art; burials; early domestication.

## Neolithic
Agriculture; animal domestication; pottery; polished tools; villages.

## Chalcolithic
Copper and stone tools; farming villages; regional cultures; pre-urban complexity.

## Pre-Harappan
Early farming, fortifications, crafts, pottery, and exchange leading toward Indus urbanism.

---

---

# 51. COMMONLY CONFUSED TERMS

## Prehistory vs Protohistory
Prehistory has no written records. Protohistory has writing or external references but unclear or undeciphered records.

## Palaeolithic vs Mesolithic
Palaeolithic used larger stone tools. Mesolithic used microliths.

## Mesolithic vs Neolithic
Mesolithic was mainly hunting-gathering with microliths. Neolithic had agriculture, domestication, pottery, and villages.

## Neolithic vs Chalcolithic
Neolithic used mainly stone tools. Chalcolithic used stone and copper tools.

## Acheulian vs Soanian
Acheulian is hand axe-cleaver tradition. Soanian is pebble-tool tradition of the north-west.

## Hand Axe vs Cleaver
Hand axe is pointed or oval bifacial tool. Cleaver has a broad straight cutting edge.

## Core Tool vs Flake Tool
Core tool is made from the main stone block. Flake tool is made from a piece struck off the core.

## Blade vs Flake
Blade is long and narrow. Flake is broader and less standardized.

## Microlith vs Celt
Microlith is tiny stone tool. Celt is polished Neolithic axe/adze.

## Pottery vs Metal
Pottery belongs to ceramic technology. Metal begins with copper in Chalcolithic contexts.

## Wild Plant Use vs Agriculture
Wild plant use means gathering natural plants. Agriculture means deliberate cultivation.

## Animal Management vs Domestication
Animal management means controlling animals to some degree. Domestication means long-term breeding and human control.

## Rock Painting vs Petroglyph
Rock painting uses pigment. Petroglyph is carved or engraved.

## Burial Goods vs Hoard
Burial goods are placed with the dead. Hoards are stored or deposited objects not necessarily linked to burial.

## Mehrgarh vs Harappa
Mehrgarh was an early farming village tradition. Harappa was a later urban civilization site.

## Pre-Harappan vs Mature Harappan
Pre-Harappan refers to early farming and regional cultures before full urban Harappan civilization. Mature Harappan refers to planned cities, seals, script, drainage, and standardization.

---

Lower Palaeolithic.

## Main Lower Palaeolithic Tools

## Main Mesolithic Features
Microliths, rock art, burials, fishing, hunting-gathering.
