## Main South Indian Neolithic Sites
Tekkalakota, Hallur, Piklihal, Utnur, Sanganakallu, Paiyampalli.

---

# 53. CHRONOLOGICAL STORY TIMELINE

## Stage 1: Geological Background
India’s prehistoric environment was shaped by the Pleistocene Ice Ages, monsoon changes, river systems, grasslands, forests, and changing animal populations.

## Stage 2: Earliest Human Presence
Early humans entered and adapted to different Indian landscapes, using rivers, stone sources, forests, and open grasslands.

## Stage 3: Lower Palaeolithic
People used large tools like hand axes and cleavers. They lived by hunting, gathering, and scavenging, often near river valleys.

## Stage 4: Middle Palaeolithic
Tool-making became more refined with flake tools, scrapers, points, and prepared-core techniques. Humans adapted to varied environments.

## Stage 5: Upper Palaeolithic
Blade tools, bone tools, ornaments, and symbolic behavior became more visible. Modern humans were present.

## Stage 6: Mesolithic
Microliths became common. People hunted smaller animals, fished, gathered many plant foods, made rock paintings, and buried their dead.

## Stage 7: Early Sedentism
Some Mesolithic groups began returning to the same places seasonally. Food storage, fishing, and local abundance encouraged longer stays.

## Stage 8: Neolithic Beginnings
People began agriculture, domesticated animals, made pottery, used polished stone tools, and lived in villages.

## Stage 9: Regional Neolithic Cultures
Different regions developed differently: Mehrgarh in the north-west, Burzahom in Kashmir, rice-using cultures in the Ganga valley, ash mound cultures in South India, and shouldered axe cultures in the north-east.

## Stage 10: Early Chalcolithic Development
Copper began to be used along with stone. Farming villages became more complex, with crafts, exchange, pottery traditions, and regional identities.

## Stage 11: Pre-Harappan Regionalization
Early farming and Chalcolithic communities in Baluchistan, Sindh, Rajasthan, Haryana, and Punjab developed fortifications, pottery styles, crafts, and trade networks.

## Stage 12: Transition toward IVC
The long prehistoric developments in agriculture, herding, craft specialization, settlement growth, and exchange created the background for the rise of the Indus Valley Civilization.

---

# 54. FINAL REVISION CLUSTER FOR UPSC PRELIMS

## Must Remember Broad Sequence
Palaeolithic → Mesolithic → Neolithic → Chalcolithic → Pre-Harappan → Indus Valley Civilization.

## Must Remember Palaeolithic Economy
Hunting, gathering, and scavenging.

## Must Remember Mesolithic Innovation
Microliths and rock art.

## Must Remember Neolithic Innovation
Agriculture, domestication, pottery, polished stone tools, and villages.

## Must Remember Chalcolithic Innovation
Use of copper with stone tools.

## Must Remember Earliest Farming Background
Mehrgarh.

## Must Remember First Major Palaeolithic Discovery
Pallavaram hand axe by Robert Bruce Foote.

## Must Remember Rock Shelter Site
Bhimbetka.

## Must Remember Kashmir Neolithic
Burzahom and Gufkral.

## Must Remember Ganga Valley Neolithic
Koldihwa, Mahagara, Lahuradewa, Chopani Mando.

## Must Remember Bihar Neolithic-Chalcolithic
Chirand and Senuwar.

## Must Remember North-East Neolithic
Daojali Hading and shouldered celts.

## Must Remember South Indian Neolithic
Ash mounds, cattle pastoralism, Tekkalakota, Hallur, Piklihal, Utnur, Sanganakallu, Paiyampalli.

## Must Remember Mesolithic Burials
Sarai Nahar Rai, Mahadaha, Damdama, Langhnaj.

## Must Remember Upper Palaeolithic Ornament
Ostrich eggshell beads at sites like Patne.

## Must Remember Narmada Fossil
Hathnora hominin fossil.

## Must Remember Toba Context
Jwalapuram preserves tools around Toba ash deposits.

## Must Remember Pre-Harappan Cultures
Mehrgarh, Amri, Kot Diji, Nal, Ravi, Hakra, Sothi-Siswal.

## One-Line Essence
Indian prehistory is the long story of human adaptation from stone-tool-using hunter-gatherers to microlithic foragers, rock artists, early farmers, herders, potters, village communities, and pre-Harappan cultures that laid the foundation for the Indus Valley Civilization.

------
# INDUS VALLEY CIVILIZATION / HARAPPAN CIVILIZATION GLOSSARY FOR UPSC PRELIMS
## Chronological Coverage: Pre-Harappan Background → Early Harappan → Mature Harappan Urban Phase → Late Harappan / Localization Phase → Legacy and UPSC Revision

> Note:
> The Indus Valley Civilization is also called the Harappan Civilization because Harappa was the first site discovered.
> Broad chronology commonly used:
> 1. Neolithic-Chalcolithic Background: c. 7000–3300 BCE
> 2. Early Harappan / Regionalization Phase: c. 3300–2600 BCE
> 3. Mature Harappan / Integration Phase: c. 2600–1900 BCE
> 4. Late Harappan / Localization Phase: c. 1900–1300 BCE or later in some regions

---

# 1. BASIC TERMS AND NAMES

## Indus Valley Civilization
A Bronze Age urban civilization that developed mainly in the Indus river system and adjoining regions. It is known for planned cities, drainage, seals, weights, trade, crafts, and standardized architecture.

## Harappan Civilization
Another name for the Indus Valley Civilization, named after Harappa, the first major site excavated.

## Indus Civilization
A shorter name for the same civilization, emphasizing the Indus river system.

## Sindhu Civilization
A name based on the Sanskrit word Sindhu, meaning the Indus River.

## Indus-Saraswati Civilization
A term sometimes used because many sites are located along the Ghaggar-Hakra river system, which some identify with the ancient Saraswati. The identification is debated.

## Bronze Age Civilization
A civilization using bronze, an alloy of copper and tin, for tools, weapons, ornaments, and objects.

## Protohistoric Civilization
A civilization that had writing but whose script has not yet been deciphered. The Harappan Civilization is protohistoric because the Indus script remains unread.

## Urban Civilization
A civilization with towns and cities, planned streets, public works, craft specialization, trade, and social organization.

## First Urbanization in India
The rise of Harappan cities is called the first urbanization of the Indian subcontinent.

## Chalcolithic
A period when people used both stone and copper tools. The pre-Harappan and early Harappan stages had Chalcolithic features.

## Integration Era
The Mature Harappan period is sometimes called the Integration Era because Harappan culture became highly standardized across a large area.

## Regionalization Era
The Early Harappan period is sometimes called the Regionalization Era because many regional cultures developed before full urban integration.

## Localization Era
The Late Harappan period is sometimes called the Localization Era because urban unity broke down and regional cultures became more distinct.

---

# 2. DISCOVERY AND EXCAVATION TERMS

## Harappa
A major Harappan site in present-day Punjab, Pakistan. It was the first site discovered and gave the civilization its name.

## Mohenjo-daro
A major Harappan city in Sindh, Pakistan. It is famous for the Great Bath, drainage, large buildings, seals, and bronze objects.

## Daya Ram Sahni
The archaeologist who excavated Harappa in the 1920s.

## R. D. Banerji
The archaeologist associated with the discovery and excavation of Mohenjo-daro.

## John Marshall
Director-General of the Archaeological Survey of India who announced the discovery of the Indus Civilization in 1924.

## Mortimer Wheeler
An archaeologist who excavated Harappa and proposed early invasion theories, which are no longer accepted in their old form.

## M. S. Vats
An archaeologist associated with Harappa excavations.

## E. J. H. Mackay
An archaeologist associated with Mohenjo-daro excavations.

## S. R. Rao
Indian archaeologist associated with excavations at Lothal.

## B. B. Lal
Indian archaeologist associated with Kalibangan and other important excavations.

## J. P. Joshi
Archaeologist associated with important Harappan discoveries, including Dholavira.

## R. S. Bisht
Archaeologist who carried out major excavations at Dholavira.

## Archaeological Survey of India
The main government body responsible for archaeological research, excavation, conservation, and heritage management in India.

## Excavation
The scientific digging of an archaeological site to uncover remains of the past.

## Stratigraphy
The study of layers at an archaeological site. Lower layers are generally older than upper layers.

## Carbon Dating
A scientific method used to estimate the age of organic materials.

## Thermoluminescence Dating
A method used to date pottery and other heated materials.

## Archaeobotany
The study of ancient plant remains, such as seeds and grains.

## Archaeozoology
The study of ancient animal remains.

## Bioarchaeology
The study of human remains from archaeological sites.

## Paleoenvironment
The ancient environment of a region, including climate, rivers, vegetation, and landscape.

---

# 3. BROAD CHRONOLOGY OF HARAPPAN CIVILIZATION

## Pre-Harappan Phase
The stage before urban Harappan civilization. It includes early farming villages and regional cultures.

## Neolithic Background
The background of settled farming, animal domestication, and village life before the rise of Harappan cities.

## Chalcolithic Background
The stage when copper and stone tools were used before the full development of Harappan urbanism.

## Early Harappan Phase
The period of growing settlements, fortifications, crafts, long-distance contacts, and regional cultures before Mature Harappan urbanism.

## Mature Harappan Phase
The fully developed urban phase, known for standardized bricks, seals, weights, planned cities, drainage, and long-distance trade.

## Late Harappan Phase
The phase after the decline of major cities. Urban features weakened and regional cultures continued.

## Post-Urban Phase
The period after the decline of Mature Harappan urbanism when smaller rural settlements became more common.

## Deurbanization
The decline of cities and urban features such as large public buildings, seals, standard weights, and long-distance trade.

## Cultural Continuity
The survival of some Harappan traditions after the decline of cities, such as pottery styles, crafts, symbols, and rural settlement patterns.

## Cultural Transformation
The change from urban Harappan culture to later regional cultures.

---

# 4. PRE-HARAPPAN BACKGROUND

## Mehrgarh
An important Neolithic-Chalcolithic site in Balochistan. It shows early farming, animal domestication, pottery, beads, and early village life.

## Kili Ghul Muhammad
An early Neolithic site in Balochistan, important for understanding early farming communities.

## Nausharo
A site in Balochistan showing transition from pre-Harappan to Harappan cultural phases.

## Mundigak
A site in Afghanistan showing cultural contacts with the broader north-western region.

## Rana Ghundai
A pre-Harappan site in Balochistan.

## Amri
An early Harappan site in Sindh with regional pottery and fortification features.

## Kot Diji
An important early Harappan site in Sindh. It shows a fortified settlement and pre-urban development.

## Rehman Dheri
An early planned settlement in present-day Pakistan, important for understanding pre-urban development.

## Bhirrana
An important site in Haryana with early cultural levels. It is often discussed in relation to the antiquity of Harappan settlement in the Ghaggar-Hakra region.

## Kunal
An early Harappan site in Haryana known for ornaments, craft evidence, and early settlement features.

## Rakhigarhi Early Levels
Early levels at Rakhigarhi show the growth of settlement before the mature urban phase.

## Hakra Ware
A pottery tradition associated with early settlements in the Ghaggar-Hakra region.

## Ravi Phase
An early cultural phase associated with Harappa and surrounding areas before the Mature Harappan period.

## Kot Diji Phase
An early Harappan cultural phase marked by fortified settlements, pottery styles, and craft development.

## Amri-Nal Culture
A regional pre-Harappan and early Harappan culture in Sindh and Balochistan.

## Nal Pottery
A colorful pottery tradition from Balochistan, associated with pre-Harappan cultures.

## Sothi-Siswal Culture
An early Harappan cultural tradition found mainly in Rajasthan, Haryana, and Punjab.

## Village Farming
The settled agricultural life that formed the base for later Harappan urbanism.

## Domestication
The process of taming plants and animals for human use.

## Early Agriculture
Cultivation of crops such as wheat and barley in pre-Harappan communities.

## Early Pastoralism
Rearing animals such as cattle, sheep, and goats before full urban development.

---

# 5. GEOGRAPHICAL EXTENT

## North-Western Subcontinent
The broad region where the Harappan Civilization developed, including modern Pakistan and north-western India.

## Indus Basin
The river system of the Indus and its tributaries. It formed the core region of many Harappan settlements.

## Ghaggar-Hakra Basin
A dried river system with many Harappan sites. It is often discussed in relation to the Saraswati debate.

## Saraswati Debate
The debate over whether the Ghaggar-Hakra river system corresponds to the Vedic Saraswati.

## Punjab
A major region of Harappan settlement, covering parts of modern India and Pakistan.

## Sindh
A core Harappan region in present-day Pakistan, containing sites like Mohenjo-daro and Chanhudaro.

## Balochistan
A region with many pre-Harappan and early Harappan sites, such as Mehrgarh and Nausharo.

## Gujarat
A major Harappan region in India, containing sites like Dholavira, Lothal, Surkotada, Rangpur, and Rojdi.

## Kachchh
A region in Gujarat where Dholavira and Surkotada are located.

## Saurashtra
A peninsula in Gujarat with many Harappan sites.

## Haryana
A major Harappan region in India, with sites such as Rakhigarhi, Banawali, Bhirrana, Farmana, and Kunal.

## Rajasthan
A Harappan region with sites such as Kalibangan and Sothi.

## Western Uttar Pradesh
The easternmost region of Harappan influence, including Alamgirpur.

## Maharashtra
The southernmost region of Harappan influence, represented by Daimabad.

## Afghanistan
Connected with Harappan trade and cultural contacts, especially for lapis lazuli and other raw materials.

## Makran Coast
The coastal region of Balochistan, important for Harappan maritime trade.

## Persian Gulf
An important trade zone connecting Harappans with Oman, Bahrain, and Mesopotamia.

---

# 6. IMPORTANT RIVERS AND WATER SYSTEMS

## Indus
The main river of the civilization. Many settlements developed along the Indus and its tributaries.

## Ravi
A tributary of the Indus. Harappa is located near the Ravi region.

## Chenab
A major tributary of the Indus.

## Jhelum
A tributary of the Indus.

## Beas
A river of the Punjab region.

## Sutlej
A river associated with the north-western region and later river shifts.

## Ghaggar
A seasonal river in India with many Harappan sites along its course.

## Hakra
The dried river channel in Pakistan corresponding to the Ghaggar system.

## Saraswati
A river mentioned in later Vedic texts. Some scholars connect it with the Ghaggar-Hakra system, but the issue is debated.

## Nara
A river/channel in Sindh region, relevant to ancient drainage systems.

## Luni
A river system in Rajasthan and Gujarat region, relevant to western settlements.

## Sabarmati
A river in Gujarat near Harappan settlements.

## Mahi
A river in western India, near some Harappan cultural zones.

## Narmada
A river south of the main Harappan zone, near later cultural interactions.

## Tapti
A river in western India, south of the main Harappan area.

## Seasonal River
A river that flows mainly during the rainy season.

## River Shift
A change in the course of a river. River shifts are often discussed as one factor in Harappan decline.

## Floodplain
Flat land near a river that gets flooded and is useful for agriculture.

## Inundation Agriculture
Agriculture based on floodwater moisture rather than canal irrigation.

---

# 7. MAJOR HARAPPAN SITES: CORE CITIES

## Harappa
A major urban site in Punjab, Pakistan. It had a citadel, lower town, granary-like structures, cemetery areas, seals, weights, and craft evidence.

## Mohenjo-daro
A large urban center in Sindh, Pakistan. It is famous for the Great Bath, drainage system, large buildings, wells, seals, and bronze Dancing Girl.

## Dholavira
A major Harappan city in Kachchh, Gujarat. It is famous for water reservoirs, stone architecture, three-part town planning, and a large signboard-like inscription.

## Rakhigarhi
One of the largest Harappan sites, located in Haryana. It shows extensive settlement, craft activities, burials, and urban features.

## Ganeriwala
A large Harappan site in Cholistan, Pakistan. It is one of the biggest known Harappan settlements but is less excavated.

## Lothal
A Harappan site in Gujarat known for its dockyard-like structure, bead-making industry, warehouse, seals, and trade links.

## Kalibangan
A Harappan site in Rajasthan known for fire altars, ploughed field evidence, fortifications, and pre-Harappan and Harappan levels.

## Banawali
A Harappan site in Haryana showing pre-Harappan and Mature Harappan phases. It has evidence of fortification and town planning.

## Chanhudaro
A Harappan site in Sindh known for bead-making, seal-making, craft production, and lack of a large citadel.

## Surkotada
A Harappan site in Kachchh, Gujarat, known for fortification and debated horse remains.

## Rangpur
A Harappan site in Gujarat showing late Harappan occupation and rice-related evidence.

## Rojdi
A Harappan site in Gujarat associated with regional Harappan settlement and agriculture.

## Daimabad
A site in Maharashtra associated with late Harappan cultural influence and bronze objects.

## Alamgirpur
An eastern Harappan site in western Uttar Pradesh. It marks the eastern limit of Harappan influence.

## Sutkagendor
A Harappan site near the Makran coast, often seen as a trading outpost.

## Sotka Koh
A coastal Harappan site in Balochistan, linked with maritime trade.

## Balakot
A coastal site in Balochistan known for shell working and trade.

## Kuntasi
A Harappan site in Gujarat, probably linked with craft production and trade.

## Nageshwar
A Harappan site in Gujarat known especially for shell-working.

## Desalpur
A Harappan site in Kachchh, Gujarat, with fortified settlement remains.

## Khirsara
A Harappan site in Kachchh with fortification and craft evidence.

## Farmana
A Harappan site in Haryana known for settlement remains and cemetery evidence.

## Balu
A Harappan site in Haryana known for plant remains and agricultural evidence.

## Mitathal
A Harappan site in Haryana showing early and mature Harappan occupation.

## Girawad
A Harappan site in Haryana.

## Ropar
A site in Punjab with Harappan and later cultural levels.

## Bhagwanpura
A site in Haryana showing late Harappan and Painted Grey Ware overlap.

---

# 8. EARLY HARAPPAN FEATURES

## Fortification
Walls built around settlements. Early Harappan sites like Kot Diji and Kalibangan show fortification.

## Regional Pottery
Different pottery styles developed in different regions before Harappan standardization.

## Craft Specialization
The development of expert crafts such as bead-making, pottery, metallurgy, and shell work.

## Long-Distance Contact
Exchange networks existed before the mature urban phase and later became more organized.

## Settlement Hierarchy
Different sizes of settlements, such as villages, towns, and larger centers.

## Proto-Urbanism
The stage before full cities, when settlements began showing planning, fortification, and specialized crafts.

## Standardization Beginnings
Early signs of uniform pottery, weights, bricks, and symbols before Mature Harappan standardization.

## Pre-Harappan Kalibangan
The early phase of Kalibangan before full Harappan urban features.

## Pre-Harappan Banawali
The early phase of Banawali before Mature Harappan planning.

## Kot Diji Fortification
Fortified early Harappan settlement showing growing social organization.

## Fire Altars at Early Kalibangan
Ritual structures found in early and mature contexts at Kalibangan.

---

# 9. MATURE HARAPPAN URBANISM

## Mature Harappan Civilization
The fully developed phase of Harappan civilization with large cities, planned streets, drainage, seals, weights, and craft specialization.

## City Planning
Organized layout of cities with streets, drains, houses, public buildings, and sometimes fortified sections.

## Grid Pattern
A layout where roads cut each other at right angles. It is seen in several Harappan cities.

## Orthogonal Planning
Planned streets arranged in a rectangular or grid-like pattern.

## Citadel
The raised fortified part of a Harappan city, usually containing important public buildings.

## Lower Town
The larger residential part of a Harappan city where ordinary people lived.

## Middle Town
A separate urban division seen especially at Dholavira.

## Bailey
A fortified area or enclosure, especially discussed in relation to Dholavira.

## Acropolis
A high or fortified part of a city. Sometimes used for the citadel area.

## Fortified Settlement
A settlement protected by walls.

## Defensive Wall
A wall built for protection, flood control, or symbolic separation.

## Mud Brick
Sun-dried brick used in many Harappan structures.

## Baked Brick
Brick fired in a kiln. It was widely used in drains, wells, bathrooms, and important structures.

## Standard Brick Ratio
Harappan bricks often followed the ratio 1:2:4 in thickness, width, and length.

## Stone Architecture
Use of stone in construction, especially important at Dholavira.

## Public Architecture
Large structures used by the community or administration, such as baths, platforms, warehouses, and reservoirs.

## Domestic Architecture
Houses, rooms, courtyards, wells, bathrooms, and kitchens used by families.

## Courtyard House
A house built around an open central courtyard.

## Private Bathroom
Many Harappan houses had bathing areas connected to drains.

## Well
A water source. Mohenjo-daro had a large number of wells.

## Public Well
A well used by a group or neighborhood.

## Drainage System
A network of drains carrying wastewater away from houses and streets.

## Covered Drain
A drain covered with bricks or stone slabs. It is a major feature of Harappan urban planning.

## Soak Pit
A pit used to collect and absorb wastewater.

## Manhole
An opening for cleaning drains.

## Street Drain
A drain running along a street.

## House Drain
A drain carrying wastewater from a house to a street drain.

## Great Bath
A large public bathing tank at Mohenjo-daro. It may have been used for ritual bathing or public ceremonies.

## Granary
A large structure believed by some scholars to have stored grain. Examples are discussed at Harappa and Mohenjo-daro, though interpretation is debated.

## Warehouse
A storage structure, such as the one identified at Lothal.

## Assembly Hall
A large pillared hall-like structure at Mohenjo-daro, possibly used for public or administrative purposes.

## Dockyard
A basin-like structure at Lothal, interpreted by many as a dockyard. Some scholars debate whether it was a dock, reservoir, or water-management structure.

## Reservoir
A large water-storage structure. Dholavira is famous for its reservoirs.

## Water Management
The organized collection, storage, and drainage of water. Dholavira is a major example.

## Stormwater Drain
A drain designed to carry rainwater.

## Dam
A structure used to control water flow. Dholavira had sophisticated water-control features.

## Stepwell-like Reservoir
A reservoir with steps for access, seen at Dholavira.

## Street Planning
The planned arrangement of roads and lanes in urban settlements.

## Main Street
A broad road in a Harappan city.

## Lane
A narrow street connecting houses and main roads.

## Platform
A raised structure used for buildings, storage, or protection from floods.

## Flood Protection
Urban features built to protect settlements from floods, especially in riverine cities.

---

# 10. HARAPPAN ECONOMY: AGRICULTURE

## Agricultural Economy
Harappans grew crops and raised animals while also practicing crafts and trade.

## Wheat
One of the main crops of the Harappans.

## Barley
A major Harappan cereal crop.

## Peas
A pulse crop grown by Harappans.

## Lentils
A pulse crop known from Harappan sites.

## Chickpea
A crop cultivated in Harappan regions.

## Sesame
An oilseed crop grown by Harappans.

## Mustard
An oilseed crop found in Harappan agricultural evidence.

## Cotton
One of the important Harappan crops. The Harappans are among the earliest known users of cotton.

## Rice
Rice evidence is found at some Harappan and late Harappan sites, especially in Gujarat and eastern regions, but it was not the main crop in the core areas.

## Millets
Small grains found in some Harappan regions, especially Gujarat.

## Date Palm
A fruit-bearing tree known in Harappan areas.

## Melon
A crop known from Harappan agricultural evidence.

## Field
Cultivated land used for crops.

## Ploughed Field
Evidence of a ploughed field was found at Kalibangan.

## Furrow
A line made in the field by a plough.

## Cross-Furrowing
A method of ploughing in two directions, seen in the Kalibangan field evidence.

## Plough
An agricultural tool used to turn soil.

## Terracotta Plough
A clay model of a plough found at some Harappan sites, indicating knowledge of plough agriculture.

## Irrigation
Artificial supply of water to fields. Direct large-scale canal irrigation evidence is limited, but water control was important.

## Flood Agriculture
Farming using moisture left by river floods.

## Inundation Farming
Agriculture based on seasonal flooding.

## Double Cropping
Growing two crops in one year. Some evidence suggests Harappans may have practiced seasonal crop variation.

## Rabi Crop
Winter crop, such as wheat and barley.

## Kharif Crop
Monsoon crop, such as millets, rice, and some pulses.

---

# 11. HARAPPAN ECONOMY: ANIMALS

## Animal Domestication
Keeping animals for food, milk, transport, labour, and other uses.

## Humped Bull
A common animal in Harappan seals and figurines. It represents the zebu cattle.

## Zebu
The humped cattle common in South Asia.

## Cow
An important domestic animal for milk, labour, and wealth.

## Bull
Used for ploughing, cart-pulling, and symbolic representation.

## Buffalo
A domesticated animal known to Harappans.

## Sheep
A domesticated animal used for meat, wool, and other products.

## Goat
A domesticated animal used for milk, meat, and hides.

## Pig
A domestic animal known in Harappan settlements.

## Dog
A domestic animal known to Harappans.

## Cat
Known in Harappan contexts.

## Camel
Known in north-western regions, though not central to Harappan life.

## Elephant
Known to Harappans and shown on seals.

## Rhinoceros
Shown on Harappan seals, indicating knowledge of wild animals.

## Tiger
Shown in Harappan imagery.

## Deer
A wild animal represented in Harappan art.

## Crocodile
Known in riverine environments and represented in some imagery.

## Fish
Important in diet and symbolism in some regions.

## Horse
Horse evidence in Harappan contexts is debated. It was not a central animal of Harappan civilization.

## Surkotada Horse Debate
Debate over whether horse remains found at Surkotada are truly of horse and what they imply.

## Unicorn
A one-horned mythical animal commonly shown on Harappan seals. It is the most frequent seal motif.

---

# 12. HARAPPAN CRAFTS AND INDUSTRIES

## Craft Specialization
The production of goods by skilled workers, such as bead-makers, potters, metalworkers, and shell workers.

## Bead-Making
A major Harappan craft involving stones like carnelian, agate, jasper, and lapis lazuli.

## Carnelian
A reddish semi-precious stone used for beads. Harappan carnelian beads were highly valued.

## Etched Carnelian Beads
Carnelian beads decorated with white designs using special chemical treatment.

## Agate
A semi-precious stone used for beads.

## Jasper
A stone used for ornaments and beads.

## Lapis Lazuli
A blue stone imported mainly from Afghanistan and used in ornaments.

## Steatite
A soft stone used to make seals and small objects.

## Faience
A glazed material used to make beads, bangles, and small ornaments.

## Shell Working
Craft using shells to make bangles, ornaments, ladles, and inlays.

## Conch Shell
A marine shell used for ornaments and ritual or decorative objects.

## Nageshwar Shell Industry
Nageshwar in Gujarat was an important shell-working site.

## Balakot Shell Industry
Balakot was important for shell working due to its coastal location.

## Metalworking
The craft of making objects from copper, bronze, gold, silver, and lead.

## Copper
A major metal used by Harappans.

## Bronze
An alloy of copper and tin used for tools, weapons, and sculptures.

## Tin
A metal mixed with copper to make bronze. It was not easily available locally and may have been imported.

## Gold
Used for ornaments, beads, and luxury objects.

## Silver
Used for ornaments and vessels.

## Lead
Used in small objects.

## Electrum
A natural alloy of gold and silver, occasionally used in ancient objects.

## Metallurgy
The science and craft of extracting and working metals.

## Alloy
A mixture of metals, such as bronze.

## Lost-Wax Technique
A method of metal casting. The bronze Dancing Girl is often associated with this technique.

## Cire Perdue
French term for the lost-wax method of casting.

## Pottery
Clay vessels made by hand or wheel and fired in kilns.

## Wheel-Made Pottery
Pottery made on a potter’s wheel.

## Red Ware
Common Harappan pottery with red surface and black designs.

## Black-on-Red Ware
Pottery painted with black designs on a red background.

## Perforated Jar
A jar with holes in its body. It may have been used for brewing, filtering, or ritual purposes.

## Goblet
A cup-like vessel found at Harappan sites.

## Dish-on-Stand
A pottery vessel with a dish mounted on a stand, commonly found in Harappan contexts.

## Storage Jar
A large jar used for storing grain, water, or other goods.

## Terracotta
Baked clay used to make figurines, toys, carts, beads, and household objects.

## Terracotta Figurine
A small baked-clay figure of humans, animals, or objects.

## Terracotta Cart
A clay toy model of a cart, showing knowledge of wheeled transport.

## Toy Cart
A small model cart, often made of terracotta.

## Spindle Whorl
A small object used in spinning thread.

## Weaving
The making of cloth from thread. Cotton weaving was known to Harappans.

## Textile
Cloth or woven fabric.

## Cotton Textile
Cloth made from cotton. Harappans were among the earliest users of cotton.

## Dyeing
Coloring cloth or materials. Evidence of textile processing is indirect.

## Ivory Working
Craft using elephant tusk to make combs, inlays, and ornaments.

## Bone Tools
Tools made from animal bones.

## Stone Tools
Blades, weights, and other tools made of stone.

## Chert Blade
A sharp blade made from chert stone.

## Rohri Chert
High-quality chert from the Rohri hills in Sindh, used for blades.

## Drill
A tool used to make holes in beads.

## Micro-Drilling
A highly skilled technique used to drill tiny holes in beads.

## Bead Furnace
A kiln or heating structure used in bead production.

## Kiln
A furnace used to fire pottery or heat materials.

---

# 13. HARAPPAN TRADE AND EXCHANGE

## Trade
Exchange of goods within Harappan regions and with outside regions.

## Internal Trade
Trade between Harappan settlements, such as exchange of raw materials, finished goods, food, and craft products.

## External Trade
Trade with regions outside the Harappan world, including Mesopotamia, Oman, Bahrain, Afghanistan, and Iran.

## Long-Distance Trade
Trade over large distances, often involving luxury goods and raw materials.

## Maritime Trade
Trade by sea. Harappans used coastal and sea routes.

## Overland Trade
Trade through land routes connecting the Indus region with Afghanistan, Iran, and Central Asia.

## Caravan Trade
Trade using groups of pack animals and traders moving overland.

## Bullock Cart
A cart pulled by bulls, used for transport.

## Boat
Used for river and sea transport.

## Dockyard at Lothal
A basin-like structure at Lothal often interpreted as a dockyard for ships or boats.

## Warehouse at Lothal
A storage structure near the dockyard-like basin, probably used for trade goods.

## Seal
An object used to mark ownership, identity, trade goods, or administrative control.

## Sealing
Clay impression made by pressing a seal onto wet clay.

## Package Sealing
Clay sealing attached to bundles, jars, or goods.

## Merchant
A person involved in trade.

## Caravan Leader
A person managing long-distance trade groups.

## Standard Weights
Uniform weights used for trade and measurement.

## Cubical Weights
Stone weights shaped like cubes, common in Harappan sites.

## Chert Weights
Weights made of chert stone.

## Binary Weight System
A system based on doubling units, such as 1, 2, 4, 8, 16, 32.

## Decimal Weight System
A system based on tens. Harappan weights show both binary and decimal tendencies.

## Measurement Scale
A marked object used for measuring length. Examples have been reported from sites like Lothal and Mohenjo-daro.

## Standardization
Uniformity in bricks, weights, seals, symbols, and pottery across the Harappan world.

## Meluhha
The Mesopotamian name generally identified with the Indus region.

## Mesopotamia
Ancient civilization in present-day Iraq. It had trade relations with the Harappans.

## Sumer
Southern Mesopotamian civilization that had contacts with the Indus region.

## Akkad
A Mesopotamian power that interacted with regions involved in Persian Gulf trade.

## Dilmun
Ancient Bahrain region, an intermediary in trade between Mesopotamia and the Indus region.

## Magan
Region usually identified with Oman, important for copper trade.

## Oman Copper
Copper from Oman may have been traded with Harappans.

## Persian Gulf Trade
Trade network connecting Harappans with Oman, Bahrain, and Mesopotamia.

## Harappan Seals in Mesopotamia
Seals or sealings of Indus type found in Mesopotamia, showing trade contact.

## Mesopotamian Texts
Texts that mention Meluhha, Dilmun, and Magan, useful for reconstructing Harappan foreign trade.

## Meluhhan Interpreter
A Mesopotamian reference suggesting people from Meluhha were present in Mesopotamia and needed translation.

## Raw Material Procurement
Obtaining resources like copper, gold, shell, stone, lapis lazuli, and timber from different regions.

## Luxury Goods
High-value items such as beads, ornaments, metals, and seals.

---

# 14. HARAPPAN RAW MATERIAL SOURCES

## Copper from Rajasthan
The Aravalli region, especially Khetri, was a source of copper.

## Khetri Copper Belt
A copper-rich region in Rajasthan connected with Harappan metallurgy.

## Copper from Balochistan
Balochistan also supplied copper and other minerals.

## Copper from Oman
Possible source of copper through maritime trade.

## Gold from Karnataka
South Indian gold sources may have supplied gold, though exact routes are debated.

## Silver from Iran and Afghanistan
Silver may have come through western trade networks.

## Lapis Lazuli from Badakhshan
Badakhshan in Afghanistan was a major source of lapis lazuli.

## Carnelian from Gujarat
Gujarat was an important source and processing center for carnelian.

## Shell from Gujarat Coast
Marine shells came from the Gujarat and Makran coasts.

## Timber
Wood used for construction, carts, boats, and fuel.

## Chert from Rohri Hills
Rohri hills in Sindh supplied chert for blades.

## Steatite Sources
Steatite was used for seals and ornaments and came from different mineral zones.

## Faience Materials
Silica-rich materials were used to make faience objects.

---

# 15. SEALS AND SEALINGS

## Seal
A small object, often square, used to make impressions. Harappan seals usually carry animal motifs and script.

## Steatite Seal
A seal made of steatite, often fired and glazed.

## Square Seal
The most common form of Harappan seal.

## Rectangular Seal
A seal shape also found in Harappan contexts.

## Button Seal
A small seal with a boss or projection at the back.

## Boss
The raised part at the back of a seal, usually with a hole for suspension.

## Seal Impression
The mark left by a seal on clay.

## Unicorn Seal
The most common Harappan seal type, showing a one-horned animal.

## Humped Bull Seal
A seal showing a powerful zebu bull.

## Elephant Seal
A seal with an elephant motif.

## Rhinoceros Seal
A seal with a rhinoceros motif.

## Tiger Seal
A seal with a tiger motif.

## Composite Animal
An animal figure made by combining parts of different animals.

## Pashupati Seal
A famous seal from Mohenjo-daro showing a horned seated figure surrounded by animals. Some interpret it as proto-Shiva, but this is debated.

## Standard Device
A mysterious object shown in front of the unicorn on many seals. Its function is unknown.

## Animal Motif
Animal figure used as a symbol on seals.

## Script on Seals
Short inscriptions found on many Harappan seals.

## Trade Seal
A seal possibly used in trade, administration, or identity marking.

## Ownership Mark
A symbol used to show ownership or control of goods.

## Administrative Control
Use of seals, weights, and standard measures to manage goods and transactions.

---

# 16. INDUS SCRIPT

## Indus Script
The writing system of the Harappans. It remains undeciphered.

## Undeciphered Script
A script that scholars have not yet been able to read with certainty.

## Pictographic Signs
Signs that look like pictures or symbols.

## Logo-Syllabic Hypothesis
The theory that the Indus script may have used signs for words and syllables.

## Sign
A written symbol in the Indus script.

## Inscription
Writing found on seals, pottery, copper tablets, ivory objects, or other materials.

## Short Inscriptions
Most Indus inscriptions are very short, often only a few signs.

## Dholavira Signboard
A large inscription found at Dholavira, possibly displayed publicly.

## Direction of Writing
Many Indus inscriptions appear to have been written from right to left, though variation exists.

## Boustrophedon
A style where writing direction alternates line by line. Some scholars discuss it for the Indus script, but evidence is limited.

## Corpus
The total collection of known Indus signs and inscriptions.

## Sign Frequency
How often a sign appears in inscriptions, used in script analysis.

## Decipherment
The process of reading an unknown script. The Indus script has not been conclusively deciphered.

## Bilingual Inscription
An inscription in two scripts or languages. No confirmed bilingual inscription exists for Indus script.

## Rosetta Stone Problem
The difficulty of deciphering Indus script because no bilingual text has been found.

## Proto-Dravidian Hypothesis
The theory that the Indus language may have been related to early Dravidian languages. It remains debated.

## Indo-Aryan Hypothesis
The theory that the Indus script may represent an Indo-Aryan language. It remains debated.

## Munda Hypothesis
The theory that the Indus language may have been related to Munda languages. It remains debated.

## Non-Linguistic Symbol Hypothesis
The view that Indus signs may not represent full language writing but symbols of identity, clan, or administration. This is debated.

---

# 17. HARAPPAN RELIGION AND BELIEFS

## Harappan Religion
The belief system of the Harappans, reconstructed from seals, figurines, structures, symbols, and burials. There are no deciphered texts.

## Mother Goddess
Female terracotta figurines often interpreted as fertility goddesses. This interpretation is possible but not certain.

## Fertility Cult
Worship or symbolic importance of fertility, crops, animals, and reproduction.

## Pashupati
A term used by some scholars for the horned figure on a seal from Mohenjo-daro. Identification with Shiva is debated.

## Proto-Shiva
The idea that some Harappan symbols may be early forms of later Shiva worship. This is not proven.

## Horned Deity
A figure with horns seen on some seals or tablets, possibly religious or symbolic.

## Yogic Posture
A seated posture seen on the Pashupati seal. Some link it with early yoga-like practice, but this is debated.

## Animal Worship
Possible religious importance of animals such as bull, tiger, elephant, rhinoceros, and serpent.

## Tree Worship
Possible worship or symbolic importance of trees, especially the pipal tree.

## Pipal Tree
A sacred tree shown in Harappan imagery and important in later Indian tradition.

## Sacred Tree Motif
A tree shown with figures or animals, possibly indicating religious significance.

## Serpent Symbolism
Snakes appear in Harappan art and may have had symbolic or religious meaning.

## Fire Altars
Ritual structures found at Kalibangan, Lothal, Banawali, and some other sites.

## Kalibangan Fire Altars
A row of fire altars found at Kalibangan, suggesting ritual activity.

## Lothal Fire Altars
Fire altar-like structures found at Lothal.

## Ritual Bathing
The Great Bath at Mohenjo-daro may suggest ritual purification through bathing.

## Great Bath Ritual Theory
The idea that the Great Bath was used for religious or ceremonial bathing.

## Amulet
A small object worn for protection or religious belief.

## Talisman
An object believed to protect the wearer.

## Swastika
A symbol found in Harappan contexts and later Indian traditions. It likely had auspicious meaning.

## Lingam Hypothesis
Some objects have been interpreted as early linga symbols, but this is debated.

## Yoni Hypothesis
Some objects have been interpreted as fertility-related symbols, but evidence is uncertain.

## No Large Temples
No clear monumental temple structure has been found in Harappan cities.

## No Royal Tombs
Unlike Egypt or Mesopotamia, Harappan civilization does not show huge royal tombs.

## No Definite Palace
No clearly identified palace has been found at Harappan sites.

## Ancestor Worship
Possible respect for ancestors, inferred from burial practices, but not clearly proven.

---

# 18. HARAPPAN ART

## Harappan Art
Art produced by Harappans, including seals, sculptures, pottery, terracotta figurines, beads, ornaments, and metal objects.

## Dancing Girl
A bronze figurine from Mohenjo-daro showing a young female figure. It is a famous example of Harappan metal art.

## Priest-King
A stone sculpture from Mohenjo-daro showing a bearded male figure wearing a patterned robe. Its identification as a priest or king is uncertain.

## Bearded Man
Another name often used for the so-called Priest-King figure.

## Male Torso
Stone torso figures found at Harappan sites, showing skill in stone sculpture.

## Mother Goddess Figurines
Terracotta female figurines, often associated with fertility symbolism.

## Animal Figurines
Terracotta or stone figures of bulls, dogs, monkeys, birds, and other animals.

## Terracotta Bull
A common Harappan figurine showing the importance of cattle.

## Toy Cart
A terracotta model showing wheeled transport and everyday life.

## Mask
Terracotta or shell objects interpreted as masks have been found in some contexts.

## Faience Ornaments
Glazed ornaments made of faience.

## Shell Bangles
Bangles made from marine shells.

## Copper Mirror
A mirror made from polished copper or bronze.

## Cosmetic Objects
Combs, mirrors, beads, and containers showing interest in personal decoration.

## Stone Sculpture
Rare but high-quality sculpture, such as the Priest-King and male torso.

## Bronze Sculpture
Metal sculpture made by casting, such as the Dancing Girl.

## Pottery Painting
Painted designs on pottery, often using black pigment on red surface.

## Geometric Motifs
Patterns such as circles, lines, triangles, and intersecting designs on pottery.

## Animal Motifs
Animal designs on seals, pottery, and figurines.

## Human Motifs
Human figures on terracotta figurines, tablets, and seals.

---

# 19. HARAPPAN TECHNOLOGY AND SCIENCE

## Standardized Bricks
Bricks made in uniform proportions, showing planning and standard control.

## Kiln Firing
Heating pottery or bricks in a kiln to make them harder.

## Metallurgical Skill
Knowledge of casting, alloying, hammering, and shaping metals.

## Bronze Casting
Making bronze objects by pouring molten metal into moulds.

## Copper Tools
Tools made from copper, including axes, chisels, knives, and fishhooks.

## Bronze Tools
Tools made from bronze, stronger than pure copper.

## Saw
A cutting tool known from Harappan contexts.

## Chisel
A tool used in woodworking, stonework, and metalwork.

## Axe
A tool used for cutting wood and other purposes.

## Spearhead
A pointed weapon or tool.

## Arrowhead
A small pointed projectile tip.

## Fishhook
A hook used for fishing.

## Needle
A small pointed tool used for sewing.

## Razor
A blade used for shaving or cutting hair.

## Weight System
A standardized system used to weigh goods.

## Measurement System
A system used to measure length, weight, and possibly volume.

## Linear Measurement
Measurement of length using marked scales.

## Ivory Scale
A measuring scale made of ivory, reported from Lothal.

## Shell Scale
A measuring object made from shell.

## Decimal Precision
Harappan measurement and weight systems show high precision.

## Hydraulic Engineering
Engineering related to water storage, drainage, and management.

## Reservoir Engineering
Water storage planning, especially visible at Dholavira.

## Drainage Engineering
Construction of covered drains, soak pits, and wastewater channels.

## Urban Sanitation
Systems for cleanliness, bathing, drainage, and waste removal.

## Standardization Technology
The ability to produce uniform bricks, weights, seals, and craft goods over a huge area.

---

# 20. HARAPPAN SOCIAL LIFE

## Urban Society
A society living in planned towns and cities with specialized occupations.

## Household
The basic domestic unit living in a house or group of rooms.

## Courtyard
An open space inside a house used for daily activities.

## Kitchen
A cooking area inside a house.

## Bathroom
A bathing area, often with a sloped floor and drain.

## Privacy in Houses
Many Harappan houses opened into side lanes rather than directly onto main streets, suggesting concern for privacy.

## Multi-Room Houses
Houses with several rooms, found in major cities.

## Two-Storeyed Houses
Some houses may have had upper floors, suggested by staircases.

## Staircase
Steps found in houses, possibly leading to upper floors or roofs.

## Household Well
A private well inside or near a house.

## Public Sanitation
Community-level drainage and waste management.

## Social Stratification
Differences in wealth and status. Harappan society had some inequality, but not massive royal display like Egypt.

## Elite
Higher-status group, possibly merchants, administrators, priests, or craft controllers.

## Common People
Ordinary residents involved in farming, crafts, trade, and domestic work.

## Craft Specialists
Workers skilled in a particular craft.

## Merchants
People involved in trade and exchange.

## Farmers
People growing crops and raising animals.

## Labourers
People involved in construction, transport, agriculture, and craft production.

## Children’s Toys
Toy carts, animal figurines, balls, and games show aspects of children’s life.

## Games
Dice, gaming pieces, and boards indicate recreational activities.

## Dice
Small objects used for games.

## Gaming Board
A board or marked surface used for games.

## Personal Ornaments
Beads, bangles, necklaces, earrings, and hair ornaments.

## Bangles
Circular ornaments worn on wrists, made of shell, terracotta, faience, copper, or stone.

## Necklace
An ornament worn around the neck, often made of beads.

## Earrings
Ornaments worn on ears.

## Hairpin
An object used to hold hair.

## Comb
An object used for grooming hair.

## Cosmetics
Substances or objects used for personal decoration.

## Kohl
Eye cosmetic, possibly used in ancient times.

## Mirror
A polished metal object used to see reflection.

## Clothing
Textiles were used, though actual cloth rarely survives.

## Cotton Cloth
Harappans used cotton, one of the earliest civilizations to do so.

## Shawl-like Garment
The Priest-King figure wears a patterned cloth over one shoulder.

## Trefoil Pattern
A three-leaf design seen on the robe of the Priest-King figure.

---

# 21. HARAPPAN POLITICAL ORGANIZATION

## Political Organization
The system by which Harappan society was governed and managed.

## Urban Administration
The management of cities, drainage, construction, trade, weights, and public facilities.

## Centralized Control
The idea that a central authority may have controlled standardization across regions.

## Decentralized Control
The idea that Harappan cities may have been governed by local elites rather than one empire.

## Corporate Organization
The theory that Harappan power may have been shared among groups like merchants, priests, and administrators.

## Priest-King Theory
The older idea that Harappans were ruled by priest-kings. This is not proven.

## Merchant Elite Theory
The idea that merchants and trade controllers may have had high status.

## No Clear Monarchy
There is no definite evidence of kings like those in Egypt or Mesopotamia.

## No Royal Palace
No structure has been clearly identified as a royal palace.

## No Monumental Royal Tomb
Harappans did not build huge royal tombs like Egyptian pyramids.

## Civic Authority
An authority responsible for maintaining urban order, drainage, standardization, and public works.

## Administrative Standardization
Uniform seals, weights, bricks, and measures suggest organized control.

---

# 22. HARAPPAN BURIALS AND MORTUARY PRACTICES

## Burial
The practice of placing the dead body in the ground.

## Cemetery
A burial area outside or near settlements.

## Cemetery R-37
A Harappan cemetery at Harappa associated with the Mature Harappan period.

## Cemetery H
A post-urban or Late Harappan cemetery culture at Harappa.

## Extended Burial
A burial where the body is laid out straight.

## Fractional Burial
A burial with only some bones or body parts.

## Pot Burial
Burial in a large pot or urn.

## Urn Burial
Burial using an urn to hold remains.

## Coffin Burial
Burial using a wooden coffin or coffin-like structure. Evidence is limited.

## Grave Goods
Objects placed with the dead, such as pottery, ornaments, or tools.

## Modest Grave Goods
Harappan burials usually have limited grave goods, suggesting less emphasis on royal afterlife display.

## Secondary Burial
Burial of bones after initial exposure or earlier burial.

## Cremation
Burning of the body. It appears more clearly in later cultures but may have existed in some Harappan or late contexts.

## Ancestor Ritual
Rituals connected with the dead or ancestors, inferred but not clearly proven.

## Harappan Skeletons
Human remains used to study health, diet, disease, and population.

## Trauma Evidence
Marks on bones that may show injury or violence, studied by bioarchaeologists.

## Dental Evidence
Teeth used to study diet, disease, and ancestry.

## DNA Studies
Scientific study of ancient genetic material to understand population history.

---

# 23. REGIONAL HARAPPAN VARIATIONS

## Core Harappan Area
The main region of Mature Harappan urban culture, especially Punjab, Sindh, Cholistan, Haryana, Rajasthan, and Gujarat.

## Peripheral Region
Areas influenced by Harappan culture but outside the main urban core.

## Gujarat Harappan
Regional Harappan culture in Gujarat, known for ports, beads, shell work, and distinctive pottery.

## Sorath Harappan
A regional Harappan tradition in Saurashtra, Gujarat.

## Sindh Harappan
Harappan culture in Sindh, including Mohenjo-daro and Chanhudaro.

## Punjab Harappan
Harappan culture in Punjab, including Harappa.

## Ghaggar-Hakra Harappan
Harappan settlements along the Ghaggar-Hakra river system.

## Cholistan Harappan
Harappan sites in the Cholistan desert region along the Hakra channel.

## Balochistan Harappan
Harappan and pre-Harappan sites in Balochistan, often connected with highland-lowland exchange.

## Coastal Harappan
Harappan settlements along the Makran and Gujarat coasts involved in maritime trade.

## Inland Harappan
Settlements located away from the coast, often focused on agriculture, crafts, and internal trade.

---

# 24. IMPORTANT SITE-SPECIFIC FEATURES

## Harappa Granary
Large brick platforms at Harappa interpreted by some as granary structures.

## Harappa Cemetery
Cemetery R-37 and Cemetery H are important for burial studies.

## Harappa Working Floors
Areas possibly used for processing grain or craft activity.

## Mohenjo-daro Great Bath
A large waterproof tank with steps, brickwork, and surrounding rooms.

## Mohenjo-daro Dancing Girl
Bronze sculpture showing metal-casting skill.

## Mohenjo-daro Priest-King
Stone sculpture of a bearded male figure.

## Mohenjo-daro Great Granary
A large structure interpreted by some as a granary, though debated.

## Mohenjo-daro College Building
A large building near the Great Bath, sometimes called a college or priestly residence, though interpretation is uncertain.

## Dholavira Reservoirs
Large water storage structures showing advanced water management.

## Dholavira Signboard
Large inscription made of Indus signs, probably displayed publicly.

## Dholavira Three-Part City
Dholavira had citadel, middle town, and lower town.

## Dholavira Stone Construction
Unlike many Harappan cities, Dholavira used stone extensively.

## Dholavira Stadium
A large open ground interpreted as a ceremonial or public area.

## Lothal Dockyard
A large basin-like structure interpreted as a dockyard or water reservoir.

## Lothal Bead Factory
Evidence of bead-making at Lothal.

## Lothal Warehouse
A storage structure associated with trade.

## Lothal Fire Altars
Ritual structures found at the site.

## Kalibangan Ploughed Field
Earliest known evidence of a ploughed field from the subcontinent.

## Kalibangan Fire Altars
A row of fire-altars suggesting ritual practices.

## Kalibangan Earthquake Evidence
Some scholars suggest earthquake-related destruction in early levels.

## Banawali Fortification
Evidence of fortified settlement planning.

## Banawali Toy Plough
A terracotta plough model indicating agricultural knowledge.

## Chanhudaro Bead Industry
Chanhudaro was an important craft production center.

## Chanhudaro Seal-Making
Evidence of seal production was found at Chanhudaro.

## Surkotada Fortification
A fortified Harappan settlement in Gujarat.

## Surkotada Horse Remains
Animal remains interpreted by some as horse; identification is debated.

## Rangpur Rice Evidence
Rice-related evidence is often associated with Rangpur and other Gujarat sites.

## Daimabad Bronze Hoard
Large bronze objects found at Daimabad, associated with late Harappan or post-Harappan contexts.

## Alamgirpur Eastern Limit
Alamgirpur marks the eastern reach of Harappan influence.

## Sutkagendor Trading Post
A coastal site likely connected with maritime trade.

## Balakot Coastal Craft Site
A coastal site known for shell working.

## Nageshwar Shell Working
A Gujarat site famous for shell industry.

## Kuntasi Craft-Trading Center
A Gujarat site associated with craft production and trade.

## Rakhigarhi Burials
Burials at Rakhigarhi are important for studying Harappan people and DNA.

## Farmana Cemetery
A cemetery site in Haryana important for burial practices.

---

# 25. HARAPPAN POTTERY TERMS

## Harappan Pottery
Pottery made by Harappans, often wheel-made and painted.

## Red Pottery
Common pottery type with red surface.

## Black Painted Pottery
Pottery decorated with black designs.

## Black-on-Red Pottery
A common Harappan pottery style.

## Reserved Slip Ware
A pottery style where designs are created by leaving areas unpainted.

## Incised Pottery
Pottery decorated by cutting or scratching designs.

## Perforated Jar
A jar with holes, possibly used for filtering or brewing.

## Dish-on-Stand
A common Harappan vessel form.

## Goblet
A small cup-like vessel.

## Storage Jar
Large vessel for storing grain, liquids, or other materials.

## Cooking Pot
Pottery used for cooking food.

## Painted Motifs
Designs painted on pottery, including animals, plants, geometric patterns, and intersecting circles.

## Pipal Leaf Motif
A leaf design linked with the pipal tree.

## Fish Motif
A fish-like design found on pottery and script signs.

## Peacock Motif
A bird motif found in some pottery designs.

## Intersecting Circles
A common geometric pattern in Harappan pottery.

---

# 26. HARAPPAN SYMBOLS AND MOTIFS

## Unicorn Motif
The most common animal motif on Harappan seals.

## Humped Bull Motif
A powerful bull figure seen on seals and figurines.

## Pipal Motif
A sacred tree symbol.

## Swastika Motif
An auspicious symbol found in Harappan contexts.

## Fish Sign
A common sign in the Indus script.

## Jar Sign
A sign resembling a jar or vessel.

## Human Figure
A figure shown on tablets, seals, or terracotta objects.

## Horned Headgear
Headgear shown on some human or deity-like figures.

## Standard Device
A mysterious object placed before the unicorn on seals.

## Composite Creature
A creature made by combining features of different animals.

## Master of Animals Motif
A figure associated with animals, sometimes linked to the Pashupati seal.

## Tree Deity Motif
A figure shown near or within a tree, possibly religious.

## Animal Procession
Rows of animals shown on some objects.

---

# 27. HARAPPAN TRANSPORT

## Bullock Cart
A cart pulled by bulls, used for transporting goods.

## Cart Track
Marks of cart movement found at some sites.

## Solid Wheel
A wheel made from solid wood or material, known from toy models.

## Spoked Wheel
Evidence for spoked wheels is uncertain in Harappan contexts.

## Boat
Used for river and sea travel.

## River Transport
Movement of goods and people along rivers.

## Sea Transport
Movement of goods by coastal or sea routes.

## Dock
A place where boats may be loaded and unloaded.

## Port
A settlement involved in maritime trade.

## Coastal Trade
Trade along the sea coast.

## Pack Animal
Animal used to carry goods.

## Oxen
Bulls or oxen used for carts and ploughs.

---

# 28. HARAPPAN FOOD AND DAILY LIFE

## Cereal Diet
Food based on grains such as wheat and barley.

## Pulse Diet
Use of pulses such as peas, lentils, and chickpeas.

## Milk Products
Milk, curd, and possibly ghee were part of the diet.

## Meat Consumption
Animal bones show that some Harappans consumed meat.

## Fish Consumption
Fish bones indicate fish was eaten in riverine and coastal areas.

## Fruits
Dates and other fruits were known.

## Oilseeds
Sesame and mustard provided oil.

## Grinding Stone
Stone used to grind grain.

## Quern
A stone tool used for grinding cereals.

## Pestle
A tool used for pounding grains or spices.

## Hearth
A fireplace used for cooking.

## Tandoor-like Oven
Ovens found at some sites may have been used for baking.

## Storage Bin
A container or space used to store food grains.

## Household Storage
Storage of grain, water, tools, and daily goods inside houses.

---

# 29. HARAPPAN HEALTH AND MEDICINE

## Disease Evidence
Human bones show evidence of disease, stress, or injury.

## Dental Disease
Teeth provide evidence of diet, cavities, and health.

## Trepanation
A surgical opening in the skull reported from some ancient Indian contexts, though not a central Harappan feature.

## Medical Knowledge
Direct evidence is limited, but some practices may be inferred from tools, plant use, and skeletal evidence.

## Sanitation
The drainage and bathing systems suggest concern for cleanliness.

## Bathing
Regular bathing was likely common in urban areas.

## Waste Disposal
Harappans used drains, soak pits, and rubbish areas to manage waste.

---

# 30. HARAPPAN ENVIRONMENT

## Monsoon
Seasonal rainfall that affected agriculture and water availability.

## Aridification
Gradual drying of climate. It is considered one factor in Harappan decline.

## Climate Change
Long-term changes in rainfall and temperature that affected settlements.

## River Drying
Reduction or disappearance of river flow, especially in the Ghaggar-Hakra system.

## River Avulsion
Sudden change in river course.

## Flooding
Repeated floods affected cities such as Mohenjo-daro.

## Earthquake
Seismic activity may have affected some settlements and river courses.

## Deforestation
Cutting of trees for fuel, bricks, and agriculture. Its scale and role are debated.

## Salinization
Increase of salt in soil, which can reduce agricultural productivity.

## Ecological Stress
Pressure on land, water, forests, and agricultural resources.

---

# 31. DECLINE OF HARAPPAN CIVILIZATION

## Decline
The weakening of Mature Harappan cities after around 1900 BCE.

## Deurbanization
The decline of urban life and planned cities.

## Ruralization
Shift from large cities to smaller rural settlements.

## Trade Decline
Reduction in long-distance trade with Mesopotamia and other regions.

## Standardization Decline
Loss of uniformity in bricks, weights, seals, and pottery.

## Script Disappearance
Indus script gradually disappeared in the Late Harappan period.

## Seal Decline
Use of classic square seals declined after the Mature Harappan phase.

## Weight System Decline
Standardized weights became less common.

## Craft Decline
Some specialized crafts declined or became regional.

## Climate Theory
The theory that climate drying and monsoon weakening contributed to decline.

## River Shift Theory
The theory that changing river courses affected settlements and agriculture.

## Flood Theory
The theory that repeated floods damaged cities like Mohenjo-daro.

## Tectonic Theory
The theory that earthquakes affected rivers and settlements.

## Trade Disruption Theory
The theory that decline in Mesopotamian trade weakened Harappan urban economy.

## Ecological Theory
The theory that overuse of resources contributed to decline.

## Aryan Invasion Theory
An older theory that invading Aryans destroyed Harappan cities. It is not accepted in its old form by most modern scholars.

## Multiple-Cause Theory
The view that Harappan decline happened due to several causes together, not one single cause.

## Gradual Decline
The decline was not sudden everywhere. Many settlements continued in changed forms.

## Regional Continuity
Different regions followed different paths after the decline of urban centers.

---

# 32. LATE HARAPPAN CULTURES

## Late Harappan
The post-urban phase after the Mature Harappan period.

## Cemetery H Culture
A late Harappan culture found at Harappa, known for distinctive pottery and burial practices.

## Jhukar Culture
A late/post-Harappan culture in Sindh.

## Rangpur Culture
A late Harappan phase in Gujarat.

## Bara Culture
A late Harappan culture found in Punjab and nearby regions.

## Ochre Coloured Pottery
A later culture in the Ganga-Yamuna region, not the same as Mature Harappan but sometimes discussed in post-Harappan context.

## OCP
Short form of Ochre Coloured Pottery.

## Copper Hoard Culture
A later culture known for copper objects in north India. It is not Mature Harappan but is important for post-Harappan chronology.

## Painted Grey Ware
A later Iron Age pottery culture associated with the Later Vedic period. It is not Harappan.

## PGW
Short form of Painted Grey Ware.

## Late Harappan Gujarat
Gujarat saw continuity of some Harappan elements after urban decline.

## Late Harappan Punjab
Punjab saw changes in pottery, settlement, and burial traditions.

## Late Harappan Haryana
Many smaller settlements continued after the mature phase.

## Late Harappan Sindh
Sindh saw cultures like Jhukar after the decline of Mohenjo-daro.

---

# 33. HARAPPAN LEGACY

## Urban Planning Legacy
Harappans showed one of the earliest examples of planned urban life in South Asia.

## Drainage Legacy
Their drainage and sanitation systems were among the most advanced of the ancient world.

## Craft Legacy
Harappan bead-making, shell work, metallurgy, and pottery influenced later traditions.

## Symbolic Legacy
Some symbols like pipal, swastika, and animal motifs continued in later Indian culture, though direct continuity must be treated carefully.

## Agricultural Legacy
Crop cultivation and rural settlement patterns continued after urban decline.

## Cotton Legacy
Harappans were among the earliest users of cotton textiles.

## Weight Tradition
Standardized weights show an early tradition of measurement and trade control.

## Water Management Legacy
Dholavira shows advanced water harvesting and storage suited to dry environments.

## Cultural Continuity Debate
The debate over how much Harappan culture continued into later Indian civilization.

## Discontinuity Debate
The debate over differences between Harappan and later Vedic cultures.

## Harappan-Vedic Relationship
A debated topic involving chronology, archaeology, language, religion, and cultural continuity.

---

# 34. COMPARISON WITH OTHER CIVILIZATIONS

## Mesopotamian Civilization
A river valley civilization in present-day Iraq. It had city-states, temples, kings, and writing.

## Egyptian Civilization
A Nile valley civilization known for pyramids, pharaohs, mummies, and hieroglyphs.

## Chinese Bronze Age
Ancient civilization in China with bronze technology and early writing.

## Harappa vs Mesopotamia
Harappa had stronger urban standardization and drainage, while Mesopotamia had clear temples, kings, and deciphered writing.

## Harappa vs Egypt
Harappa lacked huge royal monuments like pyramids, while Egypt had visible royal tombs and divine kingship.

## Harappa vs Vedic Culture
Harappan culture was urban, bronze age, script-using, and city-based. Early Vedic culture was more pastoral, oral, and tribal.

## Ziggurat
Mesopotamian temple tower. No Harappan equivalent has been clearly found.

## Pyramid
Egyptian royal tomb. No Harappan equivalent exists.

## Pharaoh
Egyptian king. No clear Harappan equivalent is known.

## City-State
A city with its own political authority, common in Mesopotamia. Harappan political structure is unclear.

---

# 35. IMPORTANT HARAPPAN OBJECTS

## Dancing Girl
Bronze figurine from Mohenjo-daro.

## Priest-King
Stone sculpture from Mohenjo-daro.

## Pashupati Seal
Seal with horned seated figure from Mohenjo-daro.

## Unicorn Seal
Most common seal type.

## Terracotta Mother Goddess
Female figurine linked with possible fertility symbolism.

## Terracotta Bull
Clay bull figurine showing cattle importance.

## Toy Cart
Terracotta model of a cart.

## Perforated Jar
Pottery vessel with holes.

## Dish-on-Stand
Common Harappan pottery form.

## Faience Beads
Glazed beads made from silica-rich material.

## Carnelian Beads
Semi-precious beads, often from Gujarat.

## Shell Bangles
Bangles made from marine shells.

## Copper Tools
Axes, chisels, knives, razors, needles, and fishhooks.

## Bronze Mirror
Personal object made of polished bronze.

## Chert Blades
Sharp stone blades, often from Rohri chert.

## Cubical Weights
Standardized stone weights.

## Ivory Scale
Measuring scale, associated especially with Lothal.

## Copper Tablets
Small copper objects with signs or motifs.

## Terracotta Cakes
Small baked-clay triangular or circular objects; their exact use is uncertain.

## Triangular Terracotta Cakes
Terracotta objects whose function is debated.

## Circular Terracotta Cakes
Round clay objects possibly used in crafts, cooking, or ritual; interpretation uncertain.

---

# 36. HARAPPAN ADMINISTRATION AND ECONOMIC CONTROL

## Seal System
Use of seals for marking goods, identity, or authority.

## Weight System
Use of standardized weights for trade and distribution.

## Brick Standardization
Uniform brick sizes showing planning and control.

## Craft Standardization
Uniform production styles across distant regions.

## Storage System
Use of warehouses, storage jars, and platforms for goods.

## Redistribution
The possible collection and distribution of goods by authorities.

## Record-Keeping
The use of seals and script may suggest some form of record-keeping.

## Trade Regulation
Control of trade through weights, seals, and standardized measures.

## Civic Planning
Organized construction of streets, drains, wells, and public structures.

## Public Works
Large community structures like drains, reservoirs, baths, and platforms.

---

# 37. HARAPPAN RELIGIOUS STRUCTURES AND ABSENCES

## No Definite Temple
No building has been conclusively identified as a temple.

## No Ziggurat
Harappan cities did not have Mesopotamian-style temple towers.

## No Pyramid
Harappans did not build pyramid-like royal tombs.

## No Palace
No clearly identified royal palace has been found.

## Ritual Tank
The Great Bath may have served as a ritual tank.

## Fire Ritual Area
Fire altars suggest ritual activity at some sites.

## Sacred Platform
Some raised structures may have had ritual or administrative use, but evidence is uncertain.

## Household Ritual
Some rituals may have been performed in houses or small spaces.

## Public Ritual
Large structures like the Great Bath may indicate community ceremonies.

---

# 38. HARAPPAN LANGUAGE AND ETHNICITY DEBATES

## Language Debate
The debate over what language or languages Harappans spoke.

## Dravidian Theory
The theory that Harappans spoke an early Dravidian language.

## Indo-Aryan Theory
The theory that Harappans spoke an Indo-Aryan language.

## Munda Theory
The theory that Harappans spoke a language related to Munda languages.

## Language Isolate Theory
The theory that Harappans spoke a language not clearly related to surviving language families.

## Multilingual Harappa
The possibility that different regions of the Harappan world spoke different languages.

## Ethnicity
The cultural or biological identity of people. Harappan ethnicity cannot be identified simply from artifacts.

## DNA Evidence
Genetic evidence used to study ancestry and population history.

## Rakhigarhi DNA
Ancient DNA from Rakhigarhi is important for debates on Harappan ancestry and population history.

## Population Continuity
The idea that some Harappan populations contributed to later South Asian populations.

## Migration Debate
Debate about movements of people before, during, and after the Harappan period.

---

# 39. HARAPPAN MILITARY AND WEAPONS

## Weapons
Harappans had tools and weapons, but no strong evidence of large-scale militarism.

## Spearhead
A pointed weapon or hunting tool.

## Arrowhead
Projectile point used with bow and arrow.

## Dagger
A short blade.

## Knife
A cutting tool.

## Axe
Tool or weapon.

## Mace Head
A heavy object possibly used as a weapon.

## Fortification
Walls may have served defense, flood protection, or symbolic control.

## Warfare Evidence
Evidence of organized warfare is limited compared to Mesopotamia or Egypt.

## No Clear Army Evidence
There is no clear evidence of a large standing army.

## No War Monuments
Harappans did not leave clear victory inscriptions or war sculptures.

---

# 40. HARAPPAN ECONOMIC TERMS FOR UPSC

## Subsistence Economy
Basic economy based on food production and survival needs.

## Surplus
Extra production beyond immediate need, supporting cities and crafts.

## Storage
Keeping grain or goods for future use.

## Distribution
Movement of goods from producers to users.

## Specialization
Workers focusing on specific tasks like bead-making or metallurgy.

## Exchange Network
System of trade and movement of goods.

## Resource Zone
A region supplying a particular raw material.

## Craft Workshop
A place where goods were produced.

## Production Center
A settlement or area specializing in production.

## Consumption Center
A place where goods were used or exchanged.

## Port Town
A town involved in maritime trade.

## Inland Town
A town located away from the coast.

## Rural Settlement
A village or small settlement focused mainly on agriculture.

## Urban Center
A large town or city with planning and specialized activities.

---

# 41. HARAPPAN SETTLEMENT HIERARCHY

## Village
Small settlement mainly involved in agriculture and animal rearing.

## Town
A medium-sized settlement with crafts and trade.

## City
A large planned urban settlement.

## Metropolitan Center
A very large city like Mohenjo-daro, Harappa, Dholavira, Rakhigarhi, or Ganeriwala.

## Regional Center
A major settlement controlling or influencing nearby smaller settlements.

## Craft Center
A settlement focused on specialized production.

## Port Center
A settlement connected with maritime trade.

## Outpost
A smaller settlement located for trade, resource collection, or strategic reasons.

## Hinterland
The rural area surrounding and supporting a city.

## Core-Periphery Model
A model explaining relations between main urban centers and surrounding regions.

---

# 42. HARAPPAN WATER MANAGEMENT

## Drainage
The system of removing wastewater.

## Covered Drains
Brick-covered drains along streets.

## Soakage Jar
A jar used to collect wastewater before it entered drains.

## Soak Pit
A pit used to absorb wastewater.

## Bathing Platform
A raised or paved area used for bathing.

## Waterproofing
Making surfaces resistant to water, as in the Great Bath.

## Bitumen
A natural tar-like material used for waterproofing in some ancient structures.

## Reservoir
A water-storage tank.

## Rainwater Harvesting
Collection and storage of rainwater.

## Channel
A passage for directing water.

## Dam
A structure to control or store water.

## Spillway
A passage to let excess water flow out.

## Step Access
Steps built to reach water in reservoirs or tanks.

## Dholavira Water System
A complex system of reservoirs, channels, dams, and drains designed for an arid region.

---

# 43. HARAPPAN ARCHITECTURAL TERMS

## Brick Platform
Raised brick structure used as a foundation or working area.

## Mud-Brick Wall
A wall made of sun-dried bricks.

## Baked-Brick Wall
A wall made of fired bricks.

## Stone Wall
Wall made of stone, especially at Dholavira.

## Foundation
The base of a building.

## Superstructure
The upper part of a building.

## Room
A built space inside a house or building.

## Courtyard
Open internal space.

## Doorway
Entrance into a room or house.

## Threshold
The base of a doorway.

## Staircase
Steps leading to another level.

## Roof
The top covering of a house, probably made with wood, reeds, mud, or other materials.

## Pillared Hall
A large building with pillars or column bases.

## Granary Platform
Raised platform interpreted as part of a storage structure.

## Bathing Tank
A tank used for bathing.

## Citadel Mound
The raised mound containing important buildings.

## Lower Mound
The lower residential mound of a city.

---

## Chanhudaro
Important bead-making and seal-making center.

## Lothal
Important bead-making and trade center.

## Nageshwar
Important shell-working center.

## Balakot
Important coastal shell-working center.

## Dholavira
Important urban center with craft, water management, and inscription evidence.

## Harappa
Major center with craft areas and administrative evidence.

## Mohenjo-daro
Large city with diverse craft activities.

## Kuntasi
Craft and trade-related site in Gujarat.

## Bagasra
A Harappan site in Gujarat associated with craft production.

## Shikarpur
A Harappan site in Gujarat associated with regional craft and settlement evidence.
